الثاني لأنه سلمه البائع الأول إلى المشتري برضاه القول السابع أنه غير صحيح صاحب الهداية وأولاده ومشايخ زماننا وعليه الفتوى أعني لا يملك المشتري بيعه من الغير كما في بيع المكره لا كالبيع الفاسد بعد القبض. وسئل الصدر عنه بأنه يجعل فاسداً ويمنع من الاسترداد بعد البيع من غيره كالفاسد وإن قضى الدين قال : هذا كبيع المشتري من المكره. قيل له : فإن أكل المشتري غلة الكرم والأرض والدار ؟ قال : حكمه حكم الزوائد في يضمنه إذا استهلكه ولا يغرم إن هلك كزوائد المغصوب. القول الثامن الجامع لبعض المحققين أنه فاسد في حق بعض الأحكام حتى ملك كل منهما الفسخ، وصحيح في حق بعض الأحكام كحل الإنزال ومنافع المبيع، ورهن في حق البعض حتى لم يملك المشتري بيعه من آخر ولا رهنه ولم يملك قطع الشجرة ولا هدم البناء وسقط الدين بهلاكه وانقسم الثمن إن دخله نقصان كما في الرهن . قلت هذا العقد مركب من العقود الثلاثة كالزرافة فيها صفة البعير والبقر والنمر جوز لحاجة الناس إليه بشرط سلامة البدلين لصاحبهما اهـ . وفي المستظرف : الزرافة حيوان عجيب الخلقة ولما كان مألوفها الشجرة خلق حيوانات الناقة الله يديها أطول من رجليها وهي ألوان عجيبة، يقال إنها متولدة ثلاثة الوحشية الضبع والبقرة الوحشية، فينزو الضبع على الناقة فتأتي بذكر، فينزو ذلك الذكر على البقرة فتتولد منه الزرافة والأصح أنه خلقة بذاته ذكر وأنثى كبقية الحيوانات. وقد فرع في البزازية فروعاً كثيرة يحتاج إليها في بيع الوفاء تركناها خوفاً من الإطالة، وينبغي أن لا يعدل في الإفتاء عن القول الجامع قوله : (فإن نقد في الثلاث صح) يعني في قولهم جميعاً وقدمنا صفة انعقاده في الابتداء إما فاسد أو موقوف كما في خيار الشرط، ولم أر ثمرة للاختلاف فإنه إذا أسقطه قبل دخول الرابع جاز اتفاقاً، وإن دخل تقرر فساده اتفاقاً. ولعل الثمرة تظهر في حل الاقدام عليه وعدمه، ويمكن أن يقال في ثبوت الملك بالقبض فمن قال بفساده أثبته قال بالوقف نفاه . ومن

قوله : (وخيار البائع يمنع خروج المبيع عن ملكه لأن تمام هذا السبب بالمراضاة فلا يتم مع الخيار فينفذ عتق البائع ولا يملك المشتري التصرف فيه وإن قبضه بإذن البائع. ودل كلامه على أن خيار المشتري يمنع خروج الثمن عن ملكه للعلة المذكورة، وأن الخيار إذا كان لهما لم يخرج المبيع عن ملك البائع ولا الثمن عن ملك المشتري. وفي البدائع : أن حكم البيع بخيار أنه لا موقوف على معنى يعرف له حكم للحال والخيار مانع من انعقاد الحكم. وفي المعراج : إلا أن السبب المنعقد في الأصل يسري إلى الزوائد المتصلة والمنفصلة لكونه محلاً له عند وجود

Book of Sale, Chapter: Option of Condition. He guarantees the increase like a trust and takes it back when paying the debt. Second: It is a valid sale by agreement of the scholars of the age due to custom; whatever the seller does of building and paying land tax is by consent, not compulsion, just as he is not compelled to abandon fulfillment and make it final; the buyer has the right to demand the price; if the house collapses, the seller is not compelled to return the price; similarly, if the sold item is a specific object and perishes, the matter is concluded and neither has any recourse against the other. And fulfillment. Al-Zayla'i mentioned that the fatwa is that it is a permissible sale giving some of its rulings, including permissibility of benefiting from it, but the buyer does not own it to sell to others; third: what Qadi Khan chose; and he said the correct view is that if it occurs with the word 'sale' it is not a pledge; then if they stipulate rescission in the contract or utter the word 'sale' with the condition of fulfillment, or utter the word 'sale' and with them this sale is non-binding, the sale is void; if they mention the sale without condition and then stipulate it on the basis of a promise, the sale is valid and fulfillment is binding; a promise may be binding due to people's need to avoid usury; thus they would rely on debt and leasing, which are not valid for vineyards, and in Bukhara long-term leasing is not possible for trees, so they were forced to sell them as wafa'. Fourth: what was said in al-'Uddah and chosen by Zahir al-Din: it is a void sale; even if they annex it to a sale it becomes attached and corrupts it, even after the session according to the correct view. Fifth: what the imams of Khwarazm chose: that if they utter an absolute sale but the buyer appoints an agent to rescind the sale when the seller brings the price, or he pledges that when he fulfills he rescinds the sale, and the price does not equal the sold item and there is gross exploitation, or the buyer puts a profit on the original capital, such as putting twenty dinars on a hundred, then it is a pledge; otherwise it is a final sale. Sixth: what Imam al-Zahid chose: that if the condition is not mentioned in the sale, it is a valid sale with respect to the buyer so that he owns possession, and a pledge with respect to the seller; thus the buyer cannot transfer his possession and ownership to another, and he is compelled to return it when he brings the debt, because it is like a giraffe, composed of sale and pledge; like many rulings have two rulings, like a gift during illness with a condition of compensation; we made it so due to people's need to avoid usury. In Balkh they would rely on debt and leasing, which are not valid for vineyards; the people of Bukhara would rely on long-term leasing, which is not possible for trees, so they were forced to sell them as wafa'; and no affair became narrow for people except that its ruling expanded. Seventh: what the author of al-Hidayah chose and his sons and the scholars of our time: it is not valid, and upon it is the fatwa, meaning the buyer cannot sell it to others, like the sale of one coerced, not like a void sale after possession. Eighth: the comprehensive view of some investigators: it is void with respect to some rulings such that each has the right to rescind, and valid with respect to some rulings such as permissibility of use and benefits, and a pledge with respect to some such that the buyer cannot sell it to another nor pledge it, and cannot cut the tree nor demolish the building, and the debt is discharged by its destruction, and the price is divided if a defect enters it as in a pledge. I say: This contract is composed of three contracts, like a giraffe having qualities of a camel, cow, and leopard; it is permitted due to people's need, with the condition that the two prices are sound for their owners. End. In al-Mustazraf: the giraffe is an animal of marvelous creation; because its familiar place is trees, it was created with the neck of a camel... It is said it is born from three wild animals: the hyena and the wild cow; the hyena mounts the camel and produces a male; that male mounts the cow and from it is born the giraffe. The correct view is that it is a creation by itself, male and female, like other animals. Many branches have been derived in al-Bazzaziyyah needed for bay' al-wafa', which we left out for fear of length. It is proper not to deviate in fatwa from the comprehensive view. His saying: (then if he pays within three it is valid) means in all their sayings; we have already presented the description of its inception, either void or suspended as in option of condition; and I have not seen a fruit of the disagreement, for if he drops it before the fourth enters, it is valid by agreement; and if it enters, its invalidity is established by agreement. Perhaps the fruit appears in the permissibility of proceeding with it or not; it can be said regarding the establishment of ownership by possession: whoever says it is void establishes it; whoever says it is suspended negates it. His saying: (and the seller's option prevents the sold item from leaving his ownership, because the completion of this cause is by mutual consent, so it is not completed with the option; thus the seller's manumission takes effect, and the buyer does not own to dispose of it even if he took possession with the seller's permission.) And his words indicate that the buyer's option prevents the price from leaving his ownership for the same cause, and that if the option belongs to both, the sold item does not leave the seller's ownership nor the price from the buyer's ownership. In al-Bada'i': that the ruling of a sale with option is that it is not suspended in the sense that it has a known ruling for the present, and the option prevents the ruling from taking effect. In al-Mi'raj: except that the cause that is valid in origin extends to connected and separate accessories because it is a locus for them when they exist. In all copies it is repeated with the previous, and it is not actually a repetition but necessitated by the reasoning of each of the two views, so ponder. End. Mu'sahhih. His saying: (and in al-Khaniyyah: offspring and earnings...) The implication of this is that separate increase...

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is a commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq, expanding on the legal nature of bay' al-wafa' (sale with right of redemption), a contract used in Hanafi practice to avoid usury. The text enumerates multiple Hanafi opinions on this contract, reflecting a spectrum from valid sale to void, with hybrid positions in between. The author begins by recalling earlier discussions: the second opinion declares it a valid sale based on custom and the parties' consent; the seller's improvements are voluntary, not compulsory. Al-Zayla'i's fatwa considers it a permissible sale but denies the buyer the right to resell. Qadi Khan is quoted deeming it a void sale if the condition is stated in the contract. The third and fourth opinions further refine these positions. The fifth opinion from Khwarazm introduces conditions of agency or pledge if gross exploitation exists. The sixth opinion by Imam al-Zahid treats it as a valid sale for the buyer but a pledge for the seller, drawing an analogy to a giraffe as a composite creature. The seventh opinion, attributed to the author of al-Hidayah and relied upon for fatwa, declares the sale invalid, barring the buyer from selling it to others. The eighth opinion, favored by the author, is a composite: it combines elements of void sale, valid sale, and pledge, applied differently to specific aspects. The author emphasizes that this contract is necessitated by people's need to avoid riba and should not be deviated from in fatwa. He then briefly addresses option of condition (khiyar al-shart), noting that the option prevents the transfer of ownership, and that disagreements among opinions have practical consequences only in specific cases like the permissibility of initiating such transactions.

Key Terms
بيع الوفاء (bay' al-wafa'): A sale with the condition that the seller can reclaim the sold item upon returning the price; used as a legal device to avoid usury.
خيار الشرط (khiyar al-shart): The option stipulated in a contract allowing one or both parties to rescind within a specified period (up to three days by default).
الزرافة (al-zirafah): Literally 'giraffe'; used metaphorically to describe a composite contract having features of sale, pledge, and void sale, likened to a giraffe having features of camel, cow, and leopard.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred view in this passage (al-qawl al-jami'), how is bay' al-wafa' classified?
A) It is a valid sale in all respects
B) It is a void sale in all respects
C) It is a pure pledge
D) It is a composite contract that is void in some respects, valid in others, and a pledge in others

Key Takeaway
Bay' al-wafa' is a composite contract (void in part, valid in part, pledge in part) necessitated by custom and the need to avoid riba, and the mufta bihi is to follow the comprehensive eighth opinion.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
8 min
Hybrid Nature of Sale with Khiyar al-Shart
Presents multiple Hanafi opinions on whether khiyar al-shart renders a sale fasid or mawquf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to differentiate the seventh and eighth opinions on khiyar al-shart and apply the comprehensive opinion to determine the buyer's rights and obligations.

Core Idea
A sale with khiyar al-shart is a hybrid contract: invalid for some rulings, valid for others, and like a pledge for still others.

In this passage, the commentator of Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents two competing views within the Hanafi school on the legal nature of a sale with khiyar al-shart (option of stipulation). The seventh opinion restricts the buyer from reselling, while the eighth opinion adopts a nuanced hybrid classification. Understanding this dispute is essential for correctly applying the rules of khiyar al-shart in practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two opinions
State the seventh opinion (from al-Hidayah) and the eighth opinion (comprehensive) regarding the legal nature of a sale with khiyar al-shart.
The seventh opinion treats the sale as invalid in the sense that the buyer cannot resell; the eighth splits rulings.
2
Locate the points of disagreement
Note the specific aspects where the opinions differ: whether the buyer can resell, whether the contract is fasid or sahih for certain rulings, and the analogy to sale of a coerced person vs. pledge.
The seventh opinion uses qiyas to bai' al-mukrah; the eighth uses a hybrid analogy.
3
Trace the thamarah al-khilaf
Identify the practical consequences of each opinion, such as the buyer's ability to resell, liability for consumption, and treatment of offspring or defects.
Under the seventh, the buyer cannot resell; under the eighth, restrictions apply similar to rahn.
4
Evaluate the tarjih
Determine why the comprehensive opinion is preferred (mufta bihi) based on the commentator's statement that 'one should not deviate from it in fatwa.'
The commentator endorses the eighth opinion as the relied-upon position.
5
Apply to a modern case
Given a scenario with khiyar al-shart, determine the buyer's rights to sell, consume benefits, and liability for loss using the comprehensive opinion.
E.g., buyer may use the item but cannot sell it without the seller's permission until the option period ends.

Book of Sales, Chapter of Option of Stipulation. And the seller's option prevents the sold item from leaving his ownership, and if the buyer takes possession, it is destroyed at its value, and the buyer's option does not prevent the invalid sale. Meaning that he chose the second because the first seller handed it over to the buyer with his consent. The seventh opinion: it is not valid, according to the author of Al-Hidayah, his sons, and the scholars of our time, and upon it is the fatwa, meaning the buyer does not own selling it to another, as in the sale of a coerced person, not like an invalid sale after possession. And al-Sadr was asked about it, that he makes it invalid and prevents recovery after sale to another, like the invalid, even if the debt is paid. He said: This is like the buyer's sale of a coerced person. It was said to him: What if the buyer consumes the produce of the vineyard, land, and house? He said: Its ruling is the ruling of increments: he guarantees it if he consumes it, and he is not liable if it perishes, like the increments of a usurped item. The eighth opinion, comprehensive by some investigators: it is invalid in some aspects, such that each has the right to rescind, and valid in some aspects, like the permissibility of enjoyment and benefits of the sold item, and a pledge in some aspects, such that the buyer may not sell it to another nor pledge it, nor may he cut the tree nor demolish the building, and the debt is discharged by its destruction, and the price is divided if it suffers a defect, as in a pledge. I say: This contract is composed of three contracts, like the giraffe which has the attributes of a camel, a cow, and a leopard, permitted due to people's need for it, with the condition of safety of both countervalues for their owners. And in Al-Mustazraf: The giraffe is a wondrous creature; its favorite tree is the tree, it has the characteristics of animals, its front legs are longer than its hind legs, and it has wonderful colors. It is said that it is produced from three wild animals: the hyena and the wild cow. The hyena mounts the she-camel and produces a male, then that male mounts the cow and from that the giraffe is produced. The correct view is that it is a creation in itself, male and female, like other animals. And in Al-Bazzaziyya, many branches were derived that are needed in bai' al-wafa, which we left for fear of lengthening. It is proper not to deviate in fatwa from the comprehensive opinion. His statement: (if he pays within three, it is valid) meaning according to all of them, and we have presented the nature of its formation initially as either invalid or suspended, as in khiyar al-shart, and I have seen no fruit of the difference, because if he drops it before entering the fourth, it is permissible by agreement, and if it enters, its unsoundness is established by agreement. Perhaps the fruit appears in the permissibility of proceeding with it or not, and it can be said regarding the establishment of ownership by possession: whoever says it is invalid affirms it, whoever says it is suspended negates it.
Source pages p. 13

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute on the legal nature of a sale with khiyar al-shart (option of stipulation). The commentator presents multiple opinions: the seventh opinion (from the author of Al-Hidayah and later scholars) holds that the sale is invalid (fasid) in the sense that the buyer cannot resell the item, analogous to the sale of a coerced person (al-mukrah). The eighth opinion, described as comprehensive (jami'), treats the contract as a hybrid: fasid in some respects (e.g., each party has the right to rescind, and the buyer cannot sell or pledge), sahih in others (e.g., enjoyment of benefits), and like a rahn (pledge) in yet others (e.g., the debt is discharged upon destruction of the item). The commentator (likely Ibn Nujaym) endorses this comprehensive view, calling it the 'comprehensive opinion' (al-qawl al-jami') and stating that one should not deviate from it in fatwa. He illustrates the hybrid nature by comparing it to a giraffe, which combines attributes of a camel, cow, and leopard. The practical fruit (thamarah) of the difference appears in the permissibility of proceeding with the contract and whether ownership is established upon possession. The passage also references bai' al-wafa (sale with right of redemption), a related transaction, and notes that many detailed rulings are found in Al-Bazzaziyya but omitted for brevity.

Key Terms
خيار الشرط (khiyar al-shart): An option stipulated in a sale contract allowing one or both parties to rescind within a specified period.
بيع الوفاء (bai' al-wafa): A sale with a right of redemption; the seller can repurchase the item upon returning the price.
البيع الفاسد (al-bai' al-fasid): A sale that is legally defective in some conditions, requiring a new contract to be valid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the comprehensive opinion (al-qawl al-jami') in the passage, which of the following best describes a sale with khiyar al-shart?
A) It is completely invalid (fasid) and the buyer cannot take possession.
B) It is a hybrid contract that is fasid in some respects, sahih in others, and like a rahn in yet others.
C) It is valid (sahih) and the buyer can sell the item immediately.
D) It is a suspended contract (mawquf) that cannot be acted upon by either party.

Key Takeaway
A sale with khiyar al-shart is a unique hybrid contract that combines elements of fasid, sahih, and rahn, and fatwa should follow the comprehensive opinion.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

Effect of Khiyar on Ownership and Possession: Foundational Rule
Core ruling on ownership and possession during khiyar

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the effect of seller's option and buyer's option on ownership of the sold item and price, and apply the rule to determine who may dispose of the item.

Core Idea
Seller's option keeps ownership with seller; buyer's option keeps price with buyer.

When a sale is concluded with a stipulation of khiyar (option), the contract is not fully effective until the option period ends. This lesson unpacks the foundational ruling: who owns what during the option period? The answer determines who can dispose of the item, who bears liability, and what happens to subsequent acts like manumission.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of khiyar discussed
The matn distinguishes between khiyar for the seller and khiyar for the buyer. Pinpoint which scenario each statement addresses.
Also note the combined case where both parties have khiyar.
2
Extract the ruling on ownership
For seller's option: the sold item does not leave his ownership. For buyer's option: the price does not leave his ownership. State the rule in your own words.
The cillah (reason) given is that the contract's completion depends on mutual consent (taradi), which is absent during the option.
3
Apply the ruling to actions
If the seller still owns the item, his manumission of it is effective. The buyer does not own the right to dispose of it even if he has possession.
Possession with permission does not transfer ownership during the option.
4
Examine the commentary's nuance
Al-Bada'i' explains that the sale is not suspended (mawquf) meaning it has no effect; rather, the option prevents the full formation of the ruling. The cause (sabab) does extend to increments.
This nuance avoids the contradiction that the contract would be completely void.

لو سلمه على وجه الاختيار، ولو حط عنه شيئاً من الثمن فعلى قياس مسألة الإبراء ينبغي أن يبطل خياره .اهـ. وقال قبله : باع بخيار فوهب ثمنه للمشتري في المدة أو ثمنه أو شرى به شيئاً من المشتري صح تصرفه وبطل خياره، ولو اشترى من غير شيئاً بذلك الثمن بطل خياره ولم يجز شراؤه اهـ. وكتبنا في الفوائد من الفائدة الرابعة أن خيار الشرط في البيع يمنع الحكم ولا يبطل البيع إلا في مسألة ما إذا شرط الخيار في بيع الفضولي فإنه مبطل البيع ولا يتوقف لأن الخيار له بدون الشرط فيكون الشرط مبطلاً؛ كذا في فروق الكرابيسي. وفيها أيضاً من الحادية والخمسين بعد المائتين : لا : يصح الإبراء عن الدين قبل لزوم أدائه إلا في مسائل فلينظر ثمة. وإذا كان الخيار للبائع فإنه يملك مطالبة المشتري بالثمن بخلاف ما إذا كان للمشتري كما في جامع الفصولين. وإن هلك في يد البائع انفسخ البيع ولا شيء عليهما كما في المطلق عنه وإن تعيب في يد البائع فهو على خياره لأن ما انتقص بغير فعله لا يكون مضموناً عليه ولكن المشتري يتخير إن شاء أخذه بجميع الثمن وإن شاء فسخ كما في البيع المطلق. وإن كان العيب بفعل البائع ينتقص البيع فيه بقدره لأن ما يحدث بفعله يكون مضموناً عليه وتسقط به حصته من الثمن؛ كذا ذكر الشارح . ثم اعلم أن الخيار إذا كان للبائع ثم أجازه فالملك للمشتري يقتصر على وقت الإجازة ولا يستند إلى وقت العقد لما في الخانية : رجل اشترى ابنه (فعدم إرثه دليل على الاقتصار) قال في النهر بعد أن ذكر قول الخانية المار إن الأولاد والأكساب الخ»: وأنت خبير
His statement: (And the seller's option prevents the sold item from leaving his ownership) because the completion of this cause is by mutual consent, so it is not completed with the option. Thus, the seller's manumission is effective, and the buyer does not own disposing of it even if he takes possession with the seller's permission. And his statement indicates that the buyer's option prevents the price from leaving his ownership for the mentioned reason, and that if the option is for both, the sold item does not leave the ownership of the seller nor the price from the ownership of the buyer. And in Al-Bada'i': The ruling of a sale with khiyar is that it is not suspended in the sense that it has a ruling for the present, and the option prevents the formation of the ruling. And in Al-Mi'raj: Except that the cause formed originally extends to connected and separate increments because it is a locus for them at the time of existence. ... His statement: (And in Al-Khaniyya, that children and earnings, etc.) The implication is that separate increments follow the same rule as the original, so if the original remains in ownership of the one with the option, he does not own the increments if the sale is ratified. And in Al-Khaniyya: Children and earnings, when the option is for the seller, follow the original: if ratified, they belong to the buyer; if rescinded, they belong to the seller. If the option is for the buyer, and they arise with the seller, the answer is similar; if they arise with the buyer, they belong to him whether the sale is completed or rescinded. It is said: this is their view (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad); as for his view (Abu Hanifa), they follow the original. And in Jami' al-Fusulayn: If the option is for the seller, and he delivers the sold item to the buyer, if he delivers it as a transfer of ownership, his option is void; not if he delivers it as a choice. And if he reduces something from the price, by analogy to the case of release (ibra'), his option should be void. And he said before it: If he sells with khiyar and then gives the price to the buyer as a gift during the period, or gives him the price, or buys something from the buyer with it, his transaction is valid and his option is void. And if he buys something from someone else with that price, his option is void and his purchase is not valid. And we wrote in Al-Fawa'id, from the fourth benefit, that khiyar al-shart in a sale prevents the ruling and does not nullify the sale except in the case when the khiyar is stipulated in a sale by a fuduli (unauthorized agent), then it nullifies the sale and does not suspend it, because the option exists for him without the condition, so the condition becomes nullifying. Likewise in Furuq al-Karabisi. And in it also from the 251st after two hundred: A release from a debt before its obligation is not valid except in cases, so let one look there. And when the option is for the seller, he has the right to demand the price from the buyer, unlike when it is for the buyer, as in Jami' al-Fusulayn. And if it perishes in the hands of the seller, the sale is dissolved and nothing is owed by either, as in the absolute [sale]. And if it is defective in the hands of the seller, he retains his option, because what is diminished without his action is not guaranteed by him, but the buyer has the choice: if he wishes, he takes it for the full price, and if he wishes, he rescinds, as in the absolute sale. And if the defect is by the seller's action, the sale is reduced by the amount of the defect, because what occurs by his action is guaranteed by him, and its share of the price is dropped. Thus mentioned the commentator. Then know that if the option is for the seller and then he ratifies, the ownership for the buyer is limited to the time of ratification and does not relate back to the time of the contract, because in Al-Khaniyya: A man bought his son from a man on the condition that the seller has the option; then the buyer died, and the seller ratified; the son becomes free and does not inherit his father. The lack of inheritance indicates limitation, but his manumission indicates relation back, otherwise he would not be free, as is not hidden. His statement: (And by the buyer's possession, it perishes at its value) because the sale is dissolved by destruction since it was suspended and there is no effect without the locus, so it remains in his hand as a purchase upon appraisal, and it carries its value. Thus in Al-Hidayah. And by 'value' in the simile and the thing compared, what is intended is the substitute; it includes fungible goods, for they are guaranteed by the like, and non-fungible goods are those guaranteed [by value]. The [increments] that are born like children do not prevent return, and the buyer retains the option with them, which contradicts what will come in the explanation of his statement 'and the contract is completed' where it mentions that they prevent it by agreement. And similarly, it will come shortly in the explanation of his statement 'like its being defective'. His statement: (So his lack of inheritance indicates limitation) said in Al-Nahr after mentioning the statement of Al-Khaniyya about children and earnings, 'And you are aware...'
Source pages p. 13

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq regarding the effects of khiyar al-shart on ownership and possession. The commentator explains that the seller's option prevents the sold item from leaving his ownership; thus, the seller may still exercise rights like manumission, and the buyer cannot dispose of the item even if he takes possession with permission. Conversely, the buyer's option prevents the price from leaving his ownership. If both parties have an option, both the item and the price remain in the ownership of their respective owners. The ruling is based on the principle that the contract is fully completed only by mutual consent (taradi), which is lacking as long as the option remains. The commentator cites Al-Bada'i' and Al-Mi'raj to explain that the contract does not produce its full effect immediately (it is not mawquf in the sense of having no effect; rather, the option prevents the effect). However, the cause (sabab) of the contract does extend to increments (zawa'id), both connected and separate, because they are the locus of the contract. The passage then discusses several scenarios from Al-Khaniyya: during the seller's option, offspring and earnings follow the original item—if the sale is ratified, they go to the buyer; if rescinded, to the seller. For the buyer's option, if increments arise with the seller, the same rule applies; if they arise with the buyer, they belong to the buyer regardless of ratification. This is the view of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad; but on Abu Hanifa's view, increments always follow the original. Further rulings include: if the seller delivers the item with intent to transfer ownership, his option is void; a gift of the price to the buyer voids the option; and the option prevents the full effect but does not nullify the sale except in the case of a fuduli (unauthorized agent) where the condition itself is nullifying. The passage also covers liability: if the item perishes in the seller's hands, the sale dissolves with no liability; if it is lost in the buyer's hands, the buyer is liable for its value because he holds it as a purchase on appraisal (sawm al-shira'). Defects caused by the seller reduce the price proportionally. Finally, the commentator addresses whether ownership relates back: if the seller ratifies after the buyer's death, the buyer's ownership is only from the time of ratification, so the buyer's son does not inherit (since he was not owned by the deceased buyer), but the son is freed because the ratification retroactively makes the buyer the owner at the time of the contract? Actually, the case shows that the son is freed but does not inherit, indicating a compromise: ownership begins from ratification but the cause (the contract) has some retroactive effect for manumission. The passage ends with a note that the ruling on increments being born after the option period will be discussed later.

Key Terms
الخيار (al-khiyar): Option or right to rescind a contract within a specified period.
المراضاة (al-murada'ah): Mutual consent.
المبيع (al-mabi'): The sold item.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a sale with the seller's option, can the seller validly manumit a slave that is the subject of the sale during the option period?
A) No, because the slave is not fully owned by the seller.
B) Yes, because the seller still owns the slave during the option.
C) Yes, but only if the buyer agrees.
D) No, because manumission requires full ownership which is suspended.

Key Takeaway
The seller's option keeps ownership of the sold item with the seller; the buyer's option keeps ownership of the price with the buyer.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate

Increments (Zawa'id) During Khiyar: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on increments during khiyar

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to differentiate the views of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding increments (zawa'id) generated during the option period, and apply the ruling based on who holds the option and where increments arise.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa: increments follow the original. Sahibayn: it depends on possession and the party holding the option.

During the option period, the sold item may produce increments like offspring, fruit, or earnings. Who owns these increments if the sale is later rescinded? This lesson unpacks the intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani. The answer hinges on whether the option is for the seller or the buyer, and where the increments arise.

Guided Path
1
Identify the source of the dispute
The passage refers to Al-Khaniyya and states that there is a difference between Abu Hanifa and the two companions (Sahibayn). Recognize which position is attributed to whom.
The Sahibayn's view is presented first; Abu Hanifa's view is mentioned as 'أما على قوله' (as for his view).
2
Extract the Sahibayn's ruling for seller's option
When the option is for the seller, increments follow the original: if the sale is ratified, increments go to the buyer; if rescinded, to the seller.
This is straightforward: increments are part of the original.
3
Extract the Sahibayn's ruling for buyer's option
If the option is for the buyer, and increments arise with the seller, the same rule applies. If increments arise with the buyer (i.e., after the buyer has taken possession), they belong to the buyer regardless of ratification.
This is the key difference: possession with option gives the buyer ownership of increments.
4
Contrast with Abu Hanifa's view
Abu Hanifa holds that increments always follow the original, regardless of where they arise or who holds the option.
This means even if the buyer has possession and option, increments generated in his hands would still go to the seller if the sale is rescinded.
5
Note the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: under Abu Hanifa, a buyer with option may lose increments he generated if the sale is rescinded; under Sahibayn, he keeps them, which is more equitable.
The passage does not explicitly prefer one, but the detailed treatment of Sahibayn suggests they are more commonly relied upon.

الفضولي فإنه مبطل البيع ولا يتوقف لأن الخيار له بدون الشرط فيكون الشرط مبطلاً؛ كذا في فروق الكرابيسي. وفيها أيضاً من الحادية والخمسين بعد المائتين : لا : يصح الإبراء عن الدين قبل لزوم أدائه إلا في مسائل فلينظر ثمة. وإذا كان الخيار للبائع فإنه يملك مطالبة المشتري بالثمن بخلاف ما إذا كان للمشتري كما في جامع الفصولين. وإن هلك في يد البائع انفسخ البيع ولا شيء عليهما كما في المطلق عنه وإن تعيب في يد البائع فهو على خياره لأن ما انتقص بغير فعله لا يكون مضموناً عليه ولكن المشتري يتخير إن شاء أخذه بجميع الثمن وإن شاء فسخ كما في البيع المطلق. وإن كان العيب بفعل البائع ينتقص البيع فيه بقدره لأن ما يحدث بفعله يكون مضموناً عليه وتسقط به حصته من الثمن؛ كذا ذكر الشارح . ثم اعلم أن الخيار إذا كان للبائع ثم أجازه فالملك للمشتري يقتصر على وقت الإجازة ولا يستند إلى وقت العقد لما في الخانية : رجل اشترى ابنه من رجل على أن البائع بالخيار ثم مات المشتري فأجاز البائع عتق الابن ولا يرث أباه .اهـ. فعدم إرثه دليل على الاقتصار ولكن عتقه يدل على الاستناد وإلا لم يعتق كما لا يخفى . قوله : ( وبقبض المشتري يهلك بالقيمة لأن البيع ينفسخ بالهلاك لأنه كان موقوفاً ولا نفاذ بدون المحل فبقي مقبوضاً بيده على سوم الشراء وفيه القيمة ؛ كذا في الهداية. والمراد بالقيمة في المشبه والمشبه به البدل يشمل المثلي فإنه مضمون بالمثل والقيمي هو المضمون المتولدة كالأولاد لا تمنع الرد ويبقى الخيار للمشتري معها وهو مخالف لما سيأتي في شرح قوله «وتم العقد» حيث ذكر أنها تمنعه اتفاقاً، وكذا سيأتي قريباً في شرح قوله كتعيبه) . قوله: (فعدم إرثه دليل على الاقتصار) قال في النهر بعد أن ذكر قول الخانية المار إن الأولاد والأكساب الخ»: وأنت خبير
His statement: (And the seller's option prevents the sold item from leaving his ownership) because the completion of this cause is by mutual consent, so it is not completed with the option. Thus, the seller's manumission is effective, and the buyer does not own disposing of it even if he takes possession with the seller's permission. And his statement indicates that the buyer's option prevents the price from leaving his ownership for the mentioned reason, and that if the option is for both, the sold item does not leave the ownership of the seller nor the price from the ownership of the buyer. And in Al-Bada'i': The ruling of a sale with khiyar is that it is not suspended in the sense that it has a ruling for the present, and the option prevents the formation of the ruling. And in Al-Mi'raj: Except that the cause formed originally extends to connected and separate increments because it is a locus for them at the time of existence. ... His statement: (And in Al-Khaniyya, that children and earnings, etc.) The implication is that separate increments follow the same rule as the original, so if the original remains in ownership of the one with the option, he does not own the increments if the sale is ratified. And in Al-Khaniyya: Children and earnings, when the option is for the seller, follow the original: if ratified, they belong to the buyer; if rescinded, they belong to the seller. If the option is for the buyer, and they arise with the seller, the answer is similar; if they arise with the buyer, they belong to him whether the sale is completed or rescinded. It is said: this is their view (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad); as for his view (Abu Hanifa), they follow the original. And in Jami' al-Fusulayn: If the option is for the seller, and he delivers the sold item to the buyer, if he delivers it as a transfer of ownership, his option is void; not if he delivers it as a choice. And if he reduces something from the price, by analogy to the case of release (ibra'), his option should be void. And he said before it: If he sells with khiyar and then gives the price to the buyer as a gift during the period, or gives him the price, or buys something from the buyer with it, his transaction is valid and his option is void. And if he buys something from someone else with that price, his option is void and his purchase is not valid. And we wrote in Al-Fawa'id, from the fourth benefit, that khiyar al-shart in a sale prevents the ruling and does not nullify the sale except in the case when the khiyar is stipulated in a sale by a fuduli (unauthorized agent), then it nullifies the sale and does not suspend it, because the option exists for him without the condition, so the condition becomes nullifying. Likewise in Furuq al-Karabisi. And in it also from the 251st after two hundred: A release from a debt before its obligation is not valid except in cases, so let one look there. And when the option is for the seller, he has the right to demand the price from the buyer, unlike when it is for the buyer, as in Jami' al-Fusulayn. And if it perishes in the hands of the seller, the sale is dissolved and nothing is owed by either, as in the absolute [sale]. And if it is defective in the hands of the seller, he retains his option, because what is diminished without his action is not guaranteed by him, but the buyer has the choice: if he wishes, he takes it for the full price, and if he wishes, he rescinds, as in the absolute sale. And if the defect is by the seller's action, the sale is reduced by the amount of the defect, because what occurs by his action is guaranteed by him, and its share of the price is dropped. Thus mentioned the commentator. Then know that if the option is for the seller and then he ratifies, the ownership for the buyer is limited to the time of ratification and does not relate back to the time of the contract, because in Al-Khaniyya: A man bought his son from a man on the condition that the seller has the option; then the buyer died, and the seller ratified; the son becomes free and does not inherit his father. The lack of inheritance indicates limitation, but his manumission indicates relation back, otherwise he would not be free, as is not hidden. His statement: (And by the buyer's possession, it perishes at its value) because the sale is dissolved by destruction since it was suspended and there is no effect without the locus, so it remains in his hand as a purchase upon appraisal, and it carries its value. Thus in Al-Hidayah. And by 'value' in the simile and the thing compared, what is intended is the substitute; it includes fungible goods, for they are guaranteed by the like, and non-fungible goods are those guaranteed [by value]. The [increments] that are born like children do not prevent return, and the buyer retains the option with them, which contradicts what will come in the explanation of his statement 'and the contract is completed' where it mentions that they prevent it by agreement. And similarly, it will come shortly in the explanation of his statement 'like its being defective'. His statement: (So his lack of inheritance indicates limitation) said in Al-Nahr after mentioning the statement of Al-Khaniyya about children and earnings, 'And you are aware...'
Source pages p. 13

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq regarding the effects of khiyar al-shart on ownership and possession. The commentator explains that the seller's option prevents the sold item from leaving his ownership; thus, the seller may still exercise rights like manumission, and the buyer cannot dispose of the item even if he takes possession with permission. Conversely, the buyer's option prevents the price from leaving his ownership. If both parties have an option, both the item and the price remain in the ownership of their respective owners. The ruling is based on the principle that the contract is fully completed only by mutual consent (taradi), which is lacking as long as the option remains. The commentator cites Al-Bada'i' and Al-Mi'raj to explain that the contract does not produce its full effect immediately (it is not mawquf in the sense of having no effect; rather, the option prevents the effect). However, the cause (sabab) of the contract does extend to increments (zawa'id), both connected and separate, because they are the locus of the contract. The passage then discusses several scenarios from Al-Khaniyya: during the seller's option, offspring and earnings follow the original item—if the sale is ratified, they go to the buyer; if rescinded, to the seller. For the buyer's option, if increments arise with the seller, the same rule applies; if they arise with the buyer, they belong to the buyer regardless of ratification. This is the view of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad; but on Abu Hanifa's view, increments always follow the original. Further rulings include: if the seller delivers the item with intent to transfer ownership, his option is void; a gift of the price to the buyer voids the option; and the option prevents the full effect but does not nullify the sale except in the case of a fuduli (unauthorized agent) where the condition itself is nullifying. The passage also covers liability: if the item perishes in the seller's hands, the sale dissolves with no liability; if it is lost in the buyer's hands, the buyer is liable for its value because he holds it as a purchase on appraisal (sawm al-shira'). Defects caused by the seller reduce the price proportionally. Finally, the commentator addresses whether ownership relates back: if the seller ratifies after the buyer's death, the buyer's ownership is only from the time of ratification, so the buyer's son does not inherit (since he was not owned by the deceased buyer), but the son is freed because the ratification retroactively makes the buyer the owner at the time of the contract? Actually, the case shows that the son is freed but does not inherit, indicating a compromise: ownership begins from ratification but the cause (the contract) has some retroactive effect for manumission. The passage ends with a note that the ruling on increments being born after the option period will be discussed later.

Key Terms
الزوائد (al-zawa'id): Increments arising from the sold item, such as offspring, fruit, or earnings, during the option period.
الأولاد والأكساب (al-awlad wa al-aksab): Children and earnings; examples of increments.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Sahibayn, if the buyer has the option and increments arise with the seller, what is the ruling?
A) Increments belong to the buyer regardless.
B) Increments follow the original: if sale is ratified, they go to buyer; if rescinded, to seller.
C) Increments belong to the seller regardless.
D) Increments are divided equally.

Key Takeaway
Increments during khiyar: follow the original under Abu Hanifa; depend on possession and option party under the Sahibayn.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
advanced
12 min
Liability, Destruction, and Ratification During Khiyar
Liability, defects, and retroactivity during khiyar

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rules of liability for destruction and defect during khiyar, and explain why ratification does not relate back to the contract time for inheritance but does for manumission.

Core Idea
Destruction in seller's hands dissolves sale without liability; in buyer's hands, buyer pays value; ratification does not retroact for inheritance.

The option period can see the sold item perishing, being defective, or the option being exercised. This lesson covers the liability regimes: who bears loss when the item is destroyed or damaged, and how ratification interacts with ownership. It also addresses the apparent contradiction between manumission and inheritance regarding retroactivity.

Guided Path
1
Understand liability for destruction in seller's hands
If the item perishes while in the seller's possession (before delivery), the sale dissolves and no one owes anything.
This is because the contract was not fully effective, and the seller's possession is not a guarantee.
2
Understand liability for destruction in buyer's hands
If the buyer has possession (with permission) and the item perishes, he is liable for its value. This is based on the principle of 'sawm al-shira' (purchase upon appraisal).
The value means either the price for fungible goods or the market value for non-fungibles.
3
Apply defect rules
If a defect occurs without the seller's action, the buyer has the option to take the item at full price or rescind. If the seller causes the defect, the price is reduced proportionally.
This mirrors the absolute sale rules, but the option gives additional flexibility.
4
Analyze the effect of ratification on ownership
When the seller ratifies after the option, ownership transfers to the buyer only from the time of ratification, not retroactively. Evidence: the son of a deceased buyer does not inherit the item because the item was not owned by the father at the time of death.
However, manumission of a slave who is the subject of the sale is effective upon ratification, suggesting some retroactive effect for certain purposes.
5
Resolve the apparent contradiction
The commentator notes that manumission relates back (since it is a act of worship that takes immediate effect when the condition is fulfilled), but inheritance does not, because ownership is strictly from the time of transfer.
This is a nuanced distinction in Hanafi law.

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq regarding the effects of khiyar al-shart on ownership and possession. The commentator explains that the seller's option prevents the sold item from leaving his ownership; thus, the seller may still exercise rights like manumission, and the buyer cannot dispose of the item even if he takes possession with permission. Conversely, the buyer's option prevents the price from leaving his ownership. If both parties have an option, both the item and the price remain in the ownership of their respective owners. The ruling is based on the principle that the contract is fully completed only by mutual consent (taradi), which is lacking as long as the option remains. The commentator cites Al-Bada'i' and Al-Mi'raj to explain that the contract does not produce its full effect immediately (it is not mawquf in the sense of having no effect; rather, the option prevents the effect). However, the cause (sabab) of the contract does extend to increments (zawa'id), both connected and separate, because they are the locus of the contract. The passage then discusses several scenarios from Al-Khaniyya: during the seller's option, offspring and earnings follow the original item—if the sale is ratified, they go to the buyer; if rescinded, to the seller. For the buyer's option, if increments arise with the seller, the same rule applies; if they arise with the buyer, they belong to the buyer regardless of ratification. This is the view of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad; but on Abu Hanifa's view, increments always follow the original. Further rulings include: if the seller delivers the item with intent to transfer ownership, his option is void; a gift of the price to the buyer voids the option; and the option prevents the full effect but does not nullify the sale except in the case of a fuduli (unauthorized agent) where the condition itself is nullifying. The passage also covers liability: if the item perishes in the seller's hands, the sale dissolves with no liability; if it is lost in the buyer's hands, the buyer is liable for its value because he holds it as a purchase on appraisal (sawm al-shira'). Defects caused by the seller reduce the price proportionally. Finally, the commentator addresses whether ownership relates back: if the seller ratifies after the buyer's death, the buyer's ownership is only from the time of ratification, so the buyer's son does not inherit (since he was not owned by the deceased buyer), but the son is freed because the ratification retroactively makes the buyer the owner at the time of the contract? Actually, the case shows that the son is freed but does not inherit, indicating a compromise: ownership begins from ratification but the cause (the contract) has some retroactive effect for manumission. The passage ends with a note that the ruling on increments being born after the option period will be discussed later.

Key Terms
سوم الشراء (sawm al-shira'): A purchase upon appraisal; the buyer holds the item with an obligation to pay its value if it is destroyed.
الإجازة (al-ijazah): Ratification of the contract after the option period or by the party holding the option.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If the seller has the option and the item perishes in the seller's hands, what is the liability?
A) Seller must pay value to buyer.
B) Buyer must pay price to seller.
C) Sale dissolves, no liability.
D) Both share loss equally.

Key Takeaway
Liability during khiyar depends on possession: buyer liable for value if item perishes in his hands; seller not liable in his hands; ratification does not retroact for inheritance but does for manumission.

Rulings on Khiyar al-Shart: Liability, Defects, and Appendages
Commentary expanding Kanz al-Daqa'iq on khiyar al-shart rulings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rules on khiyar al-shart to determine ownership of offspring, liability for destruction, and effects of defects during the option period.

Core Idea
Appendages follow the principal unless the buyer has possession and option; destruction in buyer's hand incurs value; defects reduce price only if by seller's act.

This lesson unpacks the key rulings from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq on khiyar al-shart (stipulated option) in sales. We explore three critical areas: who owns offspring and earnings during the option period (with an intra-Hanafi dispute), what happens if the goods are destroyed or become defective, and which actions void the option. These principles are essential for correctly suspending and finalizing sale contracts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the option and its effect
Khiyar al-shart suspends the contract – it is sahih but not binding until the period ends. The ruling of the sale is conditional on the outcome.
Do not confuse suspension with invalidity.
2
Determine ownership of appendages
Apply the principle: appendages (offspring, earnings) follow the original (asl) unless the option is for the buyer and they occur in the buyer's hand. Know the dispute: Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn.
The mufta bihi view is the Sahibayn's for buyer's hand.
3
Assess liability for destruction
If goods perish in seller's hand, sale rescinds with no liability. If perished in buyer's hand, buyer pays value (qima) because it was held on approval (masakeer al-shira).
The concept of qima vs. mithl is key.
4
Handle defects
If defect occurs in seller's hand without his act, buyer may accept with full price or rescind. If by seller's act, price reduces proportionally.
Defects in buyer's hand follow different rules for qimi/mithli goods.
5
Recognize actions that void the option
Actions like seller delivering with intent of ownership, forgiving part of price, or buying back using the price void the option. Fuduli sale is an exception (option invalidates the sale).
Also note retroaction: ratification does not transfer ownership back to contract date.

البائع بعده للمشتري في زراعتها فزرعها تصير الأرض أمانة عند المشتري وللبائع أخذها منه متى شاء قبل أداء الثمن وليس للمشتري حبسها بالثمن لأنه لما زرعها صار كأنه سلمها إلى البائع اهـ
The condition from he absolved him? From the buyer the condition, so just as the ruling is established in the original, it is established in the appendages. End quote. Meaning, the original even if it remains in the ownership of the one who has the option, he does not own the appendages when the sale is ratified. In al-Khaniyyah: Offspring and earnings when the option is for the seller revolve with the original; if ratified they are for the buyer, if rescinded they are for the seller. If the option is for the buyer and they occur with the seller, the same answer; if they occur with the buyer, they are for him whether the sale is completed or rescinded. It was said: this is the view of the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), while on his (Abu Hanifa's) view, they revolve with the original. In Jami' al-Fusulayn: If the option is for the seller and he delivers the sold item to the buyer, if he delivers it with the intent of ownership, his option is void; not if he delivers it with the intent of choice. And if he reduces some of the price, then by analogy with the issue of absolution, his option should be void. End quote. And he said before: He sold with option and then gifted its price to the buyer during the period, or its price, or bought something from the buyer with it – his transaction is valid and his option is void. And if he bought something from someone else with that price, his option is void and his purchase is not valid. End quote. And we wrote in al-Fawa'id from the fourth benefit that khiyar al-shart in sale prevents the ruling but does not invalidate the sale except in the case when option is stipulated in a sale by a fuduli (unauthorised agent), for it invalidates the sale and does not suspend it because the option exists without the condition, so the condition becomes invalidating. As in Furuq al-Karabisi. And in it also from the two hundred fifty-first: Absolution of a debt before its obligation is due is not valid except in certain issues, so look there. And when the option is for the seller, he may demand the price from the buyer, unlike when it is for the buyer. As in Jami' al-Fusulayn. And if the goods perish in the seller's hand, the sale is rescinded and nothing is upon either of them. As in the absolute release. And if a defect occurs in the seller's hand, he retains his option because what decreases without his act is not guaranteed upon him, but the buyer chooses: if he wishes, he takes it with the full price; if he wishes, he rescinds as in an absolute sale. And if the defect is by the seller's act, the sale is reduced proportionately because what occurs by his act is guaranteed upon him, and his share of the price falls accordingly. So mentioned the commentator. Then know that when the option is for the seller and then he ratifies, ownership for the buyer is confined to the time of ratification and does not retroact to the time of contract. Because in al-Khaniyyah: A man bought his son from a man on condition that the seller has the option, then the buyer died; the seller ratified, the son was free and does not inherit his father. End quote. So his non-inheritance is evidence of confinement, but his freeing indicates retroaction, otherwise he would not be freed, as is not hidden. His statement: (And with the buyer's taking possession, it perishes with value, because the sale is rescinded by perishing since it was suspended, and it cannot be valid without the subject matter, so it remains in his hand on the basis of purchase on approval, and it carries the value.) So in al-Hidayah. And by value in the analogous and the analogized-to is meant the substitute, which includes fungible goods (mithli) as they are guaranteed by like, and non-fungible goods (qimi) as they are guaranteed by value. And the discussion here is in two places: the ruling of the analogized (which is the case of the book) and there is no difference between its perishing during the option period while it remains or after the seller rescinded the sale, as in Jami' al-Fusulayn. And if it perishes in his hand after the period without rescission therein, then it perishes with the price due to expiry of the option. In the case of the book, if the seller has the goods perish in his hand and the seller is owed the value, and the buyer claims that it ran away from his hand, the buyer's word is taken with his oath because the apparent is its life. And the sale is finalized upon the seller because upon expiry of three days, his option expires. Likewise, if the seller is the one claiming the running away and the claimant claims death, the seller's word is taken with his oath. As in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj. And the author did not mention the ruling if a defect occurs in the buyer's hand. In al-Siraj al-Wahhaj: If it is of value-goods, he must guarantee the deficiency on the day of taking possession; if it is fungible, he cannot guarantee its deficiency due to suspicion of riba. End quote. In Jami' al-Fusulayn: He sold land with option and they exchanged possession; then the seller rescinded within the period, so the land becomes guaranteed by value on the buyer, and he has the right to withhold it for the price he paid to the seller. If the seller thereafter permits the buyer to cultivate it and he does, the land becomes a trust with the buyer, and the seller may take it from him whenever he wishes before payment of the price. The buyer has no right to withhold it for the price because when he cultivated it, he became as if he delivered it to the seller. End quote.
Source pages p. 14

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is a commentary (sharh) on the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq dealing with khiyar al-shart (stipulated option) in sales. The commentator explains several key rulings: (1) The general principle that appendages (such as offspring and earnings) follow the principal during the option period, with a dispute between Abu Hanifa and the two Sahibayn. (2) Various situations that cause the option to lapse: e.g., if the seller delivers the item with the intent to transfer ownership, or if he forgives part of the price. (3) The effect of destruction: if the goods perish in the seller's hand during the option period, the sale is rescinded without liability; if they perish in the buyer's hand, the buyer is liable for the market value (qima) because he holds them as a maqbud 'ala sawm al-shira' (possession on approval basis). (4) Defect: if a defect occurs in the seller's hand without his act, the buyer has the option to accept with full price or rescind; if by seller's act, the price is reduced proportionally. (5) The critical issue of retroaction: when the option is for the seller and he later ratifies, ownership transfers only from the moment of ratification, not retroactively from the contract date, as shown by the case of a slave where the buyer died before ratification – the slave is not inherited. The passage ends with a discussion of the meaning of 'value' (qima) and the two aspects of the rule: the primary case (goods perishing during option) and the analogized case (goods taken on approval). The commentator also notes rulings on claims of loss and cultivation of land during the option period.

Key Terms
خيار الشرط (khiyar al-shart): An option stipulated in a sale contract for a fixed period (typically three days) during which either party may terminate or confirm the sale.
الأصل (al-asl): The principal good whose appendages follow it in ownership rulings.
القيمة (al-qima): Market value or substitute for non-fungible goods (qimi); as opposed to mithli (fungible) guaranteed by like.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a sale with khiyar al-shart, if the goods perish in the buyer's hand during the option period, what is the ruling?
A) The loss is shared equally.
B) The buyer must pay the value (qima) to the seller.
C) The seller bears the loss.
D) The sale is rescinded with no liability.

Key Takeaway
Khiyar al-shart suspends ownership transfer; appendage ownership, destruction liability, and defect remedies are determined by who holds the option and where the event occurs.

Liability for Goods Taken on Approval: The Condition of Naming the Price
Intra-Hanafi dispute on condition for liability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on when goods taken on approval become liable (daman), and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute over whether the price must be named by the buyer or the seller.

Core Idea
Goods on approval are a trust unless the price is named — but who must name it? The majority says the buyer, the commentator says the seller.

When a potential buyer takes goods to examine them with the intent to purchase, those goods are held as an amanah (trust). But if the goods are lost, does the buyer become liable? The Hanafi school conditionally imposes liability — the price must be mentioned. However, there is a sharp disagreement within the school: must the buyer himself speak the price, or is the seller's mention enough? This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents the established view and then a forceful critique that declares the majority position mistaken. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to evaluate competing proofs within the madhhab.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and their positions
First, distinguish the two sides: the majority (al-Faqih Abu al-Layth, al-Sadr al-Shahid, al-Tarasusi, and the zahir al-riwayah) vs. the commentator (Ibn Nujaym). The majority holds that the buyer must name the price for liability to attach. The commentator holds that the seller's naming alone is sufficient.
The text quotes al-Qunyah from Abu Hanifa as supporting the majority.
2
Analyze the proof (dalil) from al-Qunyah
Look at the hadith-like narration: The seller says 'this garment is for ten,' and the buyer says 'give it to me to look; if pleased, I will take it for ten.' Here the buyer repeats the price. The majority uses this to require the buyer's mention. The commentator interprets the buyer's taking as implied acceptance.
The example shows the buyer explicitly mentioning the price.
3
Trace the thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference)
Consider a scenario: seller says 'this is worth ten' and hands over goods; buyer takes silently and goods perish. Majority: no liability. Commentator: liable. This difference matters in everyday transactions.
The point is whether the buyer's silence can be interpreted as acceptance.
4
Evaluate the tarjih (preponderance)
The commentator declares the majority view 'خطأ' (wrong). Despite this strong language, the earlier view remains the mufta bihi in the school, as stated in al-Fatawa al-Sughra and al-Zahiriyyah. The commentary is a later critical refinement, but not the operative fatwa.
This illustrates how a later scholar can critique without overturning the established practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الثاني أعني المشبه به وهو المقبوض على سوم الشراء فأطلقه في الهداية وقيده في أكثر الكتب بأن يسمي ثمنه. وعبارة الصدر الشهيد في الفتاوى الصغرى : المقبوض على سوم الشراء إنما يكون مضموناً إذا كان الثمن مسمى؛ نص عليه الفقيه أبو الليث في بيوع العيون فإنه ذكر إذا قال اذهب بهذا الثوب فإن رضيته اشتريته فذهب به فهلك لا يضمن وإن قال إن رضيته اشتريته بعشرة فذهب به فهلك فإنه يضمن القيمة وعليه الفتوى اهـ. وفي الظهيرية أن هذا الشرط في ظاهر الرواية. وذكر الطرسوسي في أنفع الوسائل بعد ذكر منقولات فتحرر أنه مضمون أن ذكر الثمن حالة المساومة، والمراد بذكر الثمن فيه من جانب المشتري لا من جانب البائع وحده فإنه قال في القنية عن أبي حنيفة : قال له هذا الثوب بعشرة فقال هاته حتى أنظر إليه فإن رضيته أخذته بعشرة فضاع فهو على ذلك الثمن، فجعل ذكر البائع وحده ليس بموجب للضمان. وكذا في المسألة التي ذكر بعد هذه : لو قال إن رضيته أخذته بعشرة فعليه ،قيمته ولو قال صاحب الثوب هو بعشرة فقال المساوم حتى أنظر إليه وقبضه وضاع لا يلزمه شيء. فعلمنا أن المراد ذكر الثمن من جهة المساوم لا من جهة البائع وحده إلى أخر ما أطال فيه وقال : فليعتن بهذا التحرير فإنه فائدة جليلة . قلت : هو خطأ وبيان الثمن من جهة البائع وحده إذا أخذه المشتري بعده على وجه السوم كاف لضمانه . قال في الخانية : رجل طلب من رجل ثوباً ليشتري فأعطاه البائع ثلاثة أثواب فقال هذا [؟] بأن هذا يعين كونه مستنداً وبه صرح الشارح في الزوائد. وإنما لم يستند الإرث لأن العقد لا يصلح
As for the second, meaning the analogized-to case, which is the goods taken possession of on the basis of purchase on approval (sawm al-shira'), al-Hidayah left it unrestricted, but most books restricted it by requiring that he specify its price. The expression of al-Sadr al-Shahid in al-Fatawa al-Sughra: The goods taken on approval are only guaranteed if the price is named; al-Faqih Abu al-Layth stated it in Buyu' al-'Uyun, for he mentioned: If he says 'Take this garment; if you are pleased, you buy it' and he takes it and it perishes, he is not liable. But if he says 'If you are pleased, you buy it for ten' and he takes it and it perishes, then he guarantees the value, and the fatwa is upon it. End quote. In al-Zahiriyyah, this condition is in the zahir al-riwayah. And al-Tarasusi mentioned in Anfa' al-Wasa'il after citing transmitted texts that it is established that it is guaranteed if the price is mentioned at the time of bargaining, and by 'mention of the price' is meant from the side of the bargainer (musawim), not from the seller alone. For he said in al-Qunyah on the authority of Abu Hanifa: He said to him 'This garment is for ten' and he said 'Give it to me so that I may look at it; if I am pleased, I will take it for ten' and it was lost, then he is liable for that price. So he made the seller's mention alone not a cause for guarantee. Similarly in the issue mentioned after this: If he said 'If I am pleased, I will take it for ten' then he owes its value; and if the owner of the garment said 'It is for ten' and the bargainer said 'until I look at it' and took it and it was lost, nothing is required of him. So we learned that the intended meaning is the mention of the price from the side of the bargainer, not from the seller alone, to the end of his lengthy discussion. And he said: So one should pay attention to this analysis, for it is a great benefit. I said: It is wrong, and the statement of the price from the side of the seller alone, if the buyer thereafter takes it on the basis of approval, is sufficient for its guarantee. He said in al-Khaniyyah: A man asked a man for a garment to buy, so the seller gave him three garments and said this [؟] because this determines its being based, and the commentator explicitly stated it in al-Zawa'id. And only the inheritance was not based because the contract is not valid for.
Source pages p. 15

Commentary
This passage discusses the second of two issues: goods taken on approval (maqbud 'ala sawm al-shira'). The majority of Hanafi scholars (including al-Sadr al-Shahid, al-Faqih Abu al-Layth, al-Tarasusi, and the zahir al-riwayah) hold that such goods are guaranteed (mamnoon) only if the price is mentioned by the prospective buyer (musawim) himself. If the seller alone mentions the price and the buyer takes the goods without affirming the price, the buyer is not liable for loss. The commentator (likely Ibn Nujaym) critiques this view, asserting that the seller's mention alone is sufficient for liability once the buyer takes possession on approval. This is a critical refinement because the commentator declares the established school position to be mistaken. The passage also refers to a case in al-Khaniyyah where the seller gives three garments and says something (unclear) that determines the basis of liability; the commentator notes that the inheritance does not retroact because the contract is not valid for that purpose.

Key Terms
المقبوض على سوم الشراء (al-maqbud 'ala sawm al-shira'): Goods taken possession of by a potential buyer on an approval basis, i.e., to examine and decide whether to purchase.
الثمن المسمى (al-thaman al-musamma): The explicitly stated price in the context of an approval sale, which can create liability for the buyer if the goods are lost.
ضمان (daman): Liability to compensate for loss or damage, typically by paying the value (qima) or like (mithl).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority of early Hanafi scholars (including al-Faqih Abu al-Layth), what is required for goods taken on approval to become guaranteed (mamnoon)?
A) The seller must mention the price.
B) The buyer must mention the price.
C) The goods must be of significant value.
D) The buyer must sign a contract.

Key Takeaway
Goods taken on approval are a trust until the price is named; the Hanafi school predominantly requires the buyer to name the price, but a later commentator argues the seller's mention suffices.

Liability of Goods Taken on Negotiation for Purchase
Establishes that mentioning the price by either party makes the taker liable.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that mentioning the price by either party makes the taker on negotiation liable, and distinguish this from taking for mere looking (amānah).

Core Idea
If a price is mentioned by either party, the taker is liable; otherwise it's a trust.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Rā’iq clarifies a critical distinction in the Hanafi fiqh of sales: when a potential buyer takes goods to inspect or consider, is he liable if they are destroyed? The answer hinges on whether a price has been specified. The author cites numerous authoritative Hanafi texts to prove that the mere mention of the price by either the seller or the buyer transforms the possession from a trust (amānah) to a guaranteed possession (ḍamān). This lesson will walk you through the proofs, the internal debate with al-Ṭarsūsī, and the relied-upon position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of possession
First, distinguish between 'taking for mere looking' (hāt ḥattā anẓur) and 'taking for negotiation with a price mentioned' (hāt fa-in raḍītuhu akhadhtuhu bi-'asharah). The former is a trust (amānah), the latter creates liability (ḍamān).
The key is the wording used and whether a price is specified.
2
Locate the ruling on liability
The passage states that if the buyer says 'If I am pleased I will buy it for ten' and takes the goods, he is liable for the price. Similarly, if the seller says 'It is for ten' and the buyer takes it with the intention to purchase upon satisfaction, liability attaches.
The rule: mentioning the price by either party is sufficient.
3
Examine the proofs from Hanafi sources
The author quotes from al-Khulāṣah, al-Bazzāziyyah, al-Khāniyyah, al-Dhakhīrah, al-Fatāwā al-Ẓāhīriyyah, and Furūq al-Karābīsī. All confirm that when a price is mentioned, the taker is liable; when no price is mentioned, he is not.
These are all from the Zāhir al-Riwāyah and relied-upon texts.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute with al-Ṭarsūsī
Al-Ṭarsūsī argued that both parties must specify the price for liability. The author refutes this by showing that the texts explicitly rule liability when only the seller mentions the price. Ibn al-Humām famously said al-Ṭarsūsī is 'far from fiqh' on this point.
The majority (including Abū Ḥanīfah and Abū Yūsuf) holds that one party suffices.
5
Determine the muftā bihī position
The relied-upon position is that mentioning the price by either party (seller or buyer) makes the taker liable. This is supported by the preponderance of textual evidence and the verdict of later scholars.
The thamarah al-khilāf: in practice, if a seller says 'Take it for ten' and the buyer takes it saying 'Let me look', the buyer is liable.
