Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
8 min
Does the Buyer Own During the Option Period? The Hanafi Dispute (Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn)
Sharh expanding on matn statement about buyer's option not preventing ownership transfer and the intra-Hanafi dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on ownership during khiyar al-shart, identify the evidence of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn, and explain the thamarah al-khilaf in practical cases.

Core Idea
During the buyer's option, ownership is suspended: Abu Hanifa says no, the Sahibayn say yes—each with a strong proof.

The matn states: 'And the buyer's option does not prevent (the sale from exiting the seller's ownership) nor does he own.' This seemingly clear statement conceals a deep intra-Hanafi dispute. In this lesson, we unpack the reasoning of Imam Abu Hanifa and his two leading disciples, and discover how a single legal principle can yield two contradictory yet valid rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the matn ruling and the disputed point
Read the matn: 'And the buyer's option does not prevent nor does he own.' Identify that the core dispute is whether the buyer acquires ownership during the option period. Note that both sides agree the item leaves the seller's ownership.
The matn seems to say the buyer does not own, but the Sahibayn disagree.
2
Examine Abu Hanifa's proof
Abu Hanifa argues that if the buyer owned, the price and the sold item would be in one ownership, violating the principle of exchange (mu'awada). Also, the option's purpose (deliberation) would be lost if the buyer could inadvertently manumit a relative.
This is a qiyas-based proof focusing on the nature of exchange.
3
Examine the Sahibayn's proof
The Sahibayn argue that the sold item cannot be ownerless (za'il la ila malik); it must belong to someone. Since it has left the seller, it must enter the buyer's ownership. They cite this as a principle in Sharia transactions.
Their proof is based on the impossibility of ownerlessness in trade.
4
Review the rebuttals and resolution
Imam Abu Hanifa's response: ownerlessness is not impossible—cf. the sa'iba (left-to-die animal) where there is no owner. He also addresses the pre-emption argument by noting that pre-emption is based on a right to dispose, not full ownership. The text does not give a final tarjih, but fatwa often prefers the Sahibayn's view.
The rebuttals show the sophistication of Hanafi legal reasoning.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: if the buyer manumits a slave during the option, according to Abu Hanifa the manumission is invalid (he does not own), but according to the Sahibayn, it is valid. Similarly, pre-emption and other rights may be affected.
Knowing the mufta bihi is essential for issuing fatwas.

قوله: (وخيار المشتري لا يمنع ولا يملك) أي لا يمنع خروج المبيع عن ملك البائع فيخرج عن ملكه للزومه من جهة لا من خيار له، فلو أعتقه البائع لم يصح إعتاقه، ولو كان البائع حلف وقال إن بعته فهو حر فباعه بخيار للمشتري لم يعتق لخروجه عن ملكه، ولو باعه بخيار له عتق ولا يملكه المشتري عند الإمام رحمه الله تعالى لكن يصح إعتاقه ويكون إمضاء كما في الخانية وفيها باع عبداً بجارية على أن بائع العبد بالخيار ثلاثة أيام فأعتق البائع العبد في الثلاثة أيام نفذ عتقه في قولهم ويبطل البيع لأنه أعتق ملك نفسه، وإن أعتق الجارية جاز ويكون إسقاطاً للخيار ويتم ولو أعتقهما في كلام واحد نفذ عتقه لعدم الأولوية فيهما ويغرم قيمة الجارية ولا ينفذ إعتاق المشتري في العبد ولا في الجارية، ولو كان الخيار للمشتري انعكست الأحكام. اهـ. وقالا: يملكه لأنه لما ملك خرج عن البائع فلو لم يدخل في ملك المشتري يكون زائلاً لا إلى مالك ولا عهد لنا به في الشرع. ولأبي حنيفة أنه لما لم يخرج الثمن عن ملكه فلو قلنا بأنه يدخل المبيع في ملكه لاجتمع البدلان في ملك رجل واحد حكماً للمعاوضة ولا أصل له في الشرع لأن المعاوضة تقتضي المساواة ولأن الخيار شرع نظراً للمشتري ليتروى فيقف على المصلحة، فلو ثبت الملك ربما يعتق عليه من غير اختياره بأن كان قريبه فيفوت النظر وأورد على قوله لزوم السائبة ورد بأنها هي التي لا ملك فيها لأحد ولا علقة ملك والعلقة موجودة هنا، وأورد أيضاً استحقاق الشفعة بما بخيار للمشتري وهو دليل على ملكه وأجيب بأن استحقاقها لم ينحصر في الملك بل هو أو ما في معناه من كونه أحق بها تصرفاً بدليل صحة إعتاقه كاستحقاق العبد المأذون لها مع أنه لا ملك له حقيقة وهو تكلف لا يحتاج إليه لما سيأتي أن البيع ينبرم في ضمن طلب الشفعة فيثبت مقتضى تصحيحاً. ثم اعلم أن قولهما «في دليلهما ولا عهد لنا به في الشرع معناه في باب التجارة والمعاوضات فاندفع عنهما ما
And he does not own, and by its seizure it perishes with the price, like its defect. So if he bought his wife with an option, the marriage remains, and if he has intercourse with her, he has the right to return and does not own. His statement: (And what is seized on the basis of a loan negotiation) its apparent meaning is that this is other than what preceded, understood from the end of the issue that what is meant is what preceded, so 'what' in his statement 'and what is seized' is an indefinite in the sense of a pledge. His statement: (And what is seized on the basis of marriage negotiation is guaranteed) etc. Some virtuous scholars said: Its apparent meaning is that there is no difference between whether the dower is named or not. One might say this applies when the dower is named by analogy to what is seized on sale negotiation, for it is not guaranteed except after naming the price according to the fatwa. So what is seized on marriage negotiation is guaranteed if the dower is named, otherwise not. I have not seen a transmission in this matter except that the absolute expression implies absolute guarantee unless an explicit transmission to the contrary exists. And accordingly, a distinction between them is needed, for it is not guaranteed except after naming the price, similarly what is seized on pledge negotiation is not guaranteed except when he names what he will pledge according to the most correct view, so a distinction between them is also needed. He said: A distinction has appeared to me between what is seized on sale negotiation and what is seized on pledge negotiation and what is seized on marriage negotiation, which is that the dower is legally determined in itself, and that which is legally determined is legally named, and that which is legally named is considered absolutely, except that if he marries on the condition of no dower, it is valid and the dower of the like is obligatory. If he buys on the condition of no price, it is void and invalid, considering the legal naming of the dower. Therefore, what is seized on marriage negotiation is guaranteed, whether the dower is named or not, because it is legally named, so that is considered for the obligation of guarantee, unlike the price and what is pledged with, for that is not legally determined, so naming is necessary for the obligation of guarantee therein. End quote. Some virtuous scholar refuted it saying: This distinction is not apparent to me because what is seized on sale negotiation, the value is obligatory only when he names the price and the seized item perishes, because both the price and the value are substitutes for the substance. So when he names one, the other becomes obligatory. As for the dower if it is legally named, it is not of the same type as the value because the dower is a substitute for enjoyment as established, and the value is a substitute for the substance, so there is no correspondence between the dower and the value. Thus naming one does not necessitate the other because it is not of its type. So the legal naming of the dower has no bearing on the obligation of value, as is clear upon reflection. He said: What has appeared to me in the distinction is that since the objective in sale is wealth, the absence of mentioning the price is evidence that the seller only handed it to the negotiator on the basis of trust, and the negotiator only took it as such. But when he names a price, it is guaranteed with its value because when he specifies a price, the negotiation becomes a taking for the contract, so it becomes a means to the contract, so it is annexed to the reality of the contract in terms of guarantee, to prevent harm to the owner because he did not consent to its seizure except for a compensation, so the taker becomes liable for the compensation, and its original compensation is the value unless they agree on the named amount. It is explicitly stated in al-Durr from the book of Mudaraba that what is seized on sale negotiation is seized on the basis of exchange, and when he does not specify a price, his taking is not for the contract, so it cannot be annexed to it. Likewise in al-Hawashi al-Hamawiyya from the chapter of marriage. I say: What he mentioned last of the distinction is only from the sale side, but from the marriage side he did not address it, although it is the subject.
His statement: (And the buyer's option does not prevent nor does he own) meaning it does not prevent the sold item from exiting the seller's ownership, so it exits his ownership due to its binding from one aspect not from his option. So if the seller manumits it, his manumission is invalid. If the seller took an oath and said: 'If I sell it, it is free,' then he sold it with an option for the buyer, it is not freed because it exited his ownership. If he sold it with an option for himself, it becomes free and the buyer does not own it according to Imam Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him, but his manumission is valid and is considered confirmation, as in al-Khaniyya. In it: He sold a slave for a slave girl on condition that the seller of the slave has an option of three days, so the seller manumitted the slave within the three days, his manumission is effective according to their statement, and the sale becomes void because he manumitted his own property. If he manumits the slave girl, it is permissible and is a waiver of the option, and the sale is completed. If he manumits both in one statement, his manumission is effective due to lack of priority, and he pays the value of the slave girl. The buyer's manumission of the slave or slave girl is not effective. If the option is for the buyer, the rulings are reversed. End quote. They (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: He owns it because when he owns, it exits the seller, so if it does not enter the buyer's ownership, it would be without an owner, and we have no such thing in the Sharia. Abu Hanifa's proof: since the price has not exited his ownership, if we say the sold item enters his ownership, the two substitutes would be united in the ownership of one person legally for exchange, which has no basis in the Sharia because exchange requires equity. Also, the option was legislated as a consideration for the buyer to deliberate and ascertain the benefit, so if ownership is established, perhaps it would be manumitted against his will if it is a relative, thus defeating the consideration. It was objected to his statement by the example of a left-to-die animal (sa'iba) and he replied that it has no owner and no link of ownership, whereas the link exists here. It was also objected that pre-emption is established for what is sold with an option for the buyer, which is evidence of his ownership. The answer: pre-emption is not confined to ownership but is based on ownership or its equivalent, i.e., being more entitled to dispose, as evidenced by the validity of his manumission, like the entitlement of an authorized slave even though he has no real ownership. This is a forced interpretation that is unnecessary because it will come that the sale is concluded in the process of seeking pre-emption, so its effect is established as a validation. Then know that their statement in their evidence 'and we have no such thing in the Sharia' means in the chapter of trade and exchanges, so their objection is repelled.
Source pages p. 19

Commentary
This passage is a sharh (commentary) on the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq, specifically on the issue of the buyer's option (khiyar al-shart). The author, Ibn Nujaym, expands on two main areas: (1) the liability of goods held on approval (maqbud 'ala sawm al-shira'), and (2) the effect of the buyer's option on ownership and manumission. The first part discusses the guarantee (daman) of property taken for inspection or negotiation. The rule is that if the price is named, the property is guaranteed with its value if it perishes, because the transaction is akin to a sale. If no price is named, it is a trust (amanah) and not guaranteed. The author compares this to similar situations: goods taken on loan negotiation, pledge negotiation, and marriage negotiation. There is a detailed debate about whether the dower being legally determined (muqaddar shar'an) automatically imposes guarantee even without naming. Some scholars say it does, others distinguish because the dower is not a substitute for the substance but for enjoyment. The author ultimately leans toward the view that in sale, naming price creates guarantee; in marriage, the guarantee is based on the legally determined nature of the dower, but this is contested. The second part focuses on the seller's and buyer's options. The matn states: 'The buyer's option does not prevent (sale from exiting seller's ownership) nor does he own.' The commentary explains that even with the buyer's option, the sold item leaves the seller's ownership because the sale is binding from the seller's side (luzum). However, the buyer does not acquire full ownership until the option period ends and he confirms. This leads to a well-known intra-Hanafi dispute: Imam Abu Hanifa holds that the buyer does not own during the option period, while the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that he does own. The evidence revolves around whether ownership can be suspended (ta'liq) and the nature of exchange. Abu Hanifa argues that if the buyer owned, the two substitutes (price and sold item) would be in one hand, violating the principle of exchange. Also, the purpose of the option—deliberation—would be undermined if the buyer could inadvertently manumit a relative. The companions argue that the sold item cannot be ownerless; it must pass from seller to buyer. The author reconciles by noting that the buyer's ownership, if established, is incomplete (like an authorized slave's right of disposal). The pre-emption right supporting the buyer's ownership is explained away as based on something other than full ownership. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that of the companions, but the text presents both views.

Key Terms
خيار الشرط (khiyar al-shart): An option stipulated in a contract, usually for a specified period (e.g., three days), during which the party with the option may confirm or rescind the sale.
سوم (sawm): Negotiation or approval; taking possession of an item to examine it with a view to purchase, but without a final agreement on price.
ضمان (daman): Liability or guarantee; here specifically the obligation to compensate for loss or destruction of property held in one's possession.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, what is the primary reason the buyer does not own the sold item during the option period?
A) Because the price has not yet been paid
B) Because the option is for deliberation and ownership would undermine that
C) Because the seller can still cancel the sale
D) Because the item has not been delivered

Key Takeaway
The dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on ownership during khiyar al-shart illustrates the Hanafi method of balancing textual reasoning (nass) with logical analogy (qiyas) and practical consequences.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate

Ownership During Khiyar al-Shart: Imam vs. Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on ownership during khiyar al-shart

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on ownership during khiyar al-shart and evaluate the practical consequences of each view.

Core Idea
During khiyar al-shart, the buyer does not own the item—both countervalues cannot be in one ownership.

This lesson examines a key intra-Hanafi dispute: does the buyer acquire ownership of the sold item during the stipulated option period (khiyar al-shart)? Imam Abu Hanifa holds that ownership does not transfer until the option is finalized, while his two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, argue that the buyer does own the item. The passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents the proofs of each side along with subsidiary rulings on emancipation and preemption. Understanding this ikhtilaf is essential for grasping the subtle structure of sale in Hanafi fiqh, where the nature of exchange (mu'awada) and the purpose of the option (to deliberate) shape the legal relations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core dispute
State precisely which legal question is being debated: does the buyer own the sold item during the option period?
2
Examine the Sahibayn's proof
Follow the argument: if the buyer does not own it, the item becomes ownerless (sa'iba), which has no precedent in Shari'a.
Their key phrase: 'la asla lahu fi al-shar' – no basis in law.
3
Analyze Imam's counter-argument
Understand why Imam says ownership cannot transfer: (1) both price and item would be in one hand, contradicting exchange; (2) the option's purpose (deliberation) would be defeated if ownership could trigger unintended consequences like emancipation.
4
Trace the thamarah al-khilaf
Identify the practical difference: if the seller emancipates the slave during the option, does it void the sale? According to Imam, yes (he freed his own property); according to Sahibayn, no (the buyer already owned it).
5
Evaluate the responses to objections
See how the Hanafi response distinguishes the sa'iba (truly ownerless) from khiyar (legal tie exists) and how shuf'a claims are explained without requiring full ownership.
The 'no precedent' objection is confined to trade transactions.

كما في الخانية وفيها باع عبداً على أن بائع العبد بالخيار ثلاثة أيام فأعتق البائع العبد في الثلاثة أيام نفذ عتقه في قولهم ويبطل البيع لأنه أعتق ملك نفسه، وإن أعتق الجارية جاز ويكون إسقاطاً للخيار ويتم ولو أعتقهما في كلام واحد نفذ عتقه لعدم الأولوية فيهما ويغرم قيمة الجارية ولا ينفذ إعتاق المشتري في العبد ولا في الجارية، ولو كان الخيار للمشتري انعكست الأحكام .اهـ. وقالا : يملكه لأنه لما ملك خرج عن البائع فلو لم يدخل في ملك المشتري يكون زائلاً لا إلى مالك ولا عهد لنا به في الشرع . ولأبي حنيفة أنه لما لم يخرج الثمن عن ملكه فلو قلنا بأنه يدخل المبيع في ملكه لاجتمع البدلان في ملك رجل واحد حكماً للمعاوضة ولا أصل له في الشرع لأن المعاوضة تقتضي المساواة ولأن الخيار شرع نظراً للمشتري ليتروى فيقف على المصلحة، فلو ثبت الملك ربما يعتق عليه من غير اختياره بأن كان قريبه فيفوت النظر وأورد على قوله لزوم السائبة ورد بأنها هي التي لا ملك فيها لأحد ولا علقة ملك والعلقة موجودة هنا، وأورد أيضاً استحقاق الشفعة بما بخيار للمشتري وهو دليل على ملكه وأجيب بأن استحقاقها لم بيع ينحصر في الملك بل هو أو ما في معناه من كونه أحق بها تصرفاً بدليل صحة إعتاقه كاستحقاق العبد المأذون لها مع أنه لا ملك له حقيقة وهو تكلف لا يحتاج إليه لما سيأتي أن البيع ينبرم في ضمن طلب الشفعة فيثبت مقتضى تصحيحاً. ثم اعلم أن قولهما «في دليلهما ولا عهد لنا به في الشرع معناه في باب التجارة والمعاوضات فاندفع عنهما ما الفرق لأن المقبوض على سوم الشراء إنما وجبت القيمة فيه إذا سمى الثمن فيهلك المقبوض لأن كلاً من الثمن والقيمة هو بدل العين فلما سمى أحدهما وجب الآخر. وأما المهر إن كان مسمى شرعاً فليس من جنس القيمة لأن المهر بدل المتعة كما هو مقرر والقيمة بدل العين فلا مناسبة بين المهر والقيمة فلا توجب تسمية أحدهما الآخر لأنه ليس من جنسه فلا دخل لتسمية المهر شرعاً في وجوب القيمة كما لا يخفى عند التأمل . قال : والذي ظهر لي في الفرق هو أنه لما كان المقصود في البيع المال كان عدم ذكر الثمن دليلاً على أن البائع إنما دفعه للمستام على وجه الأمانة، والمستام إنما قبضه كذلك. وأما إذا سمى ثمناً فهو مضمون بالقيمة لأنه متى بين ثمناً يكون الاستيام أخذاً للعقد فيكون وسيلة العقد فالحق بحقيقة العقد في حق الضمان دفعاً للضرر عن المالك لأنه ما رضي بقبضه إلا بعوض فصار القابض ملتزماً للعوض، وعوضه الأصلي هو القيمة ما لم يصطلحا ويتفقا على المسمى. وصرح في الدر من كتاب المضاربة بأن المقبوض على سوم الشراء مقبوض على وجه المبادلة، ومتى لم يبين ثمناً لم يكن أخذه للعقد فلا يمكن إلحاقه به ؛ كذا في الحواشي الحموية من النكاح. أقول: وما ذكره آخراً من الفرق إنما هو في جانب البيع وأما في جانب النكاح فلم يتعرض له مع أنه محل الخفاء فلم يتحصل من كلامه فائدة تأمل. قوله : فينبغي أن لا يصح الرهن أيضاً) تفريع على قوله «لم يجز إبراؤه» وقوله «قلت الخ» جواب عنه .
Book of Sale, Chapter on Stipulated Option. Regarding ownership: If the seller swore and said, 'If I sell him, he is free,' then he sold him with an option for the buyer, the slave is not freed because he is out of his ownership. But if he sold him with an option for himself, he is freed, and the buyer does not own him according to Imam (Abu Hanifa), may Allah have mercy on him, but his emancipation is valid and is considered confirmation. As in al-Khaniyya: In it, he sold a slave for a slave-girl with the condition that the seller of the slave has an option for three days, then the seller emancipated the slave within the three days: his emancipation is effective according to their (the Sahibayn) opinion, and the sale is void because he emancipated his own property. If he emancipated the slave-girl, it is valid and is a dropping of the option, and the sale is complete. If he emancipated both in one statement, his emancipation is effective due to lack of priority between them, and he must pay the value of the slave-girl. The buyer's emancipation is not effective for either the slave or the slave-girl. If the option were for the buyer, the rulings would be reversed. End quote. The two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: He (the buyer) owns it because when he owned it, it left the seller's ownership; if it did not enter the buyer's ownership, it would be ownerless, and we have no precedent for that in Shari'a. Abu Hanifa argued: Since the price did not leave the buyer's ownership, if we said the sold item enters his ownership, then both countervalues would be in the ownership of one person, which is contrary to the nature of exchange, and there is no basis for this in Shari'a because exchange requires equivalence. Moreover, the option was legislated for the buyer's benefit so that he can deliberate and consider his interest; if ownership were established, he might inadvertently free the slave if the slave is a relative, thus defeating the purpose. An objection was raised regarding the case of a runaway slave (sa'iba), and it was responded that there is no ownership or legal connection there, whereas here there is a legal connection. Another objection was raised regarding the preemption right (shuf'a) in a sale with an option for the buyer, which indicates ownership, and the response was that the entitlement to shuf'a is not limited to ownership but can be based on a stronger right to disposition, as evidenced by the validity of the buyer's emancipation and the entitlement of a mukatab slave to shuf'a even though he does not truly own. This is a forced interpretation and unnecessary because, as will come, the sale is concluded within the process of claiming shuf'a, thus confirming its effect. Then know that the meaning of their statement 'we have no precedent for that in Shari'a' is specifically in trade and exchange transactions, so their objection is repelled. The distinction is that goods taken on the basis of bargaining only necessitate the payment of value when the price is specified and the goods perish, because both the price and the value are compensation for the item; when one is specified, the other is owed. As for the dower (mahr) specified in Shari'a, it is not a form of value because dower is compensation for the right of sexual enjoyment, whereas value is compensation for the item; there is no correspondence between dower and value, so specifying one does not necessitate the other. He said: What appears to me as the distinction is that since the goal in sale is wealth, the absence of mentioning the price indicates that the seller gave it to the bargainer as a trust, and the bargainer took it as such. If a price is specified, it is guaranteed by value because specifying a price makes the bargaining a step toward concluding a contract, and therefore it is attached to the contract regarding liability to prevent harm to the owner, as he only agreed to give possession in exchange for compensation, so the recipient becomes liable for the compensation, and the original compensation is the value unless they agree on a specified amount. It is explicitly stated in al-Durr from the chapter on Mudaraba that goods taken on bargaining are taken on the basis of exchange; if no price is specified, it is not considered taking for the purpose of contract, so it cannot be analogized to it. This is from the Hamawi glosses on the book of Nikah. I say: What he mentioned later as the distinction is only in the context of sale; as for marriage, he did not address it, though it is the area of difficulty, so no benefit is gained from his words. His statement 'then it is appropriate that the mortgage should also not be valid' is a corollary of his statement 'the quittance is not valid', and my statement 'I said... etc.' is a response to it.
Source pages p. 20

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses a core intra-Hanafi dispute regarding ownership during khiyar al-shart (stipulated option). The issue arises when a seller sells an item with an option for the buyer or for himself: does the buyer own the item during the option period? Imam Abu Hanifa holds that the buyer does not own the item until the option is finalized, while the two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) hold that the buyer does own it. The passage begins by presenting rulings on emancipation that follow from each position: if the seller has the option and emancipates the slave, it is effective because the slave is still his property; if the buyer has the option, the rulings are reversed. The author then explains the reasoning of each party. The Sahibayn argue that if the buyer did not own the item, it would be ownerless, which has no precedent in Shari'a. Imam Abu Hanifa responds that the price remains in the buyer's ownership, so if the sold item also entered his ownership, both countervalues would be in one hand, contradicting the nature of exchange. Furthermore, the option is meant for the buyer to deliberate; if ownership were established, he might inadvertently emancipate a relative slave. The discussion includes responses to objections: the case of the runaway slave (sa'iba) is distinguished because it is truly ownerless here there is a legal connection (the pending sale). The objection from preemption (shuf'a) is answered by stating that shuf'a can be based on a right of disposition, not necessarily ownership. The passage then discusses the meaning of 'no precedent' as limited to trade, and provides a distinction between goods taken on bargaining and khiyar, comparing with marriage dower. This detailed analysis shows the sophistication of Hanafi legal reasoning.

Key Terms
خيار الشرط (khiyar al-shart): A stipulated option in a sale contract allowing either party to cancel within a specified period.
ملك (milk): Ownership as a legal relation with full rights of disposition.
السائبة (al-sa'iba): A runaway slave or an animal set free without ownership.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, why does the buyer not own the sold item during khiyar al-shart?
A) Because the seller still possesses the item.
B) Because both countervalues would be in one ownership, contradicting exchange.
C) Because the option period is too short to transfer ownership.
D) Because the buyer has not paid the price yet.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school (Imam) holds that durante khiyar al-shart, the buyer does not own the item; this protects the nature of exchange and the purpose of the option.

Liability for Crimes During Khiyar al-Shart: Intra-Hanafi Dispute and Ancillary Rulings
Rulings on slave's crime, corpse liability, and ancillary khiyar issues.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi dispute on blood money liability when a corpse is found during khiyar and apply the rules for jinaaya of a slave during the option period.

Core Idea
Liability during khiyar depends on possession (Imam) or eventual ownership (Sahibayn) — a key intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.

This lesson examines a nuanced area of khiyar al-shart: how liability for crimes and defects interacts with the option period. The central dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and the two Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) concerns who bears blood money when a corpse is found in a property sold with option. Understanding this ikhtilaf and the accompanying rulings on maintenance, increments, and quittance is essential for correctly applying the law in option contracts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main dispute
Locate the passage where the author states the difference between Imam and Sahibayn regarding corpse found in a house sold with khiyar. Note that Imam places liability on the 'aqila of the person in possession, while the Sahibayn place it on the eventual owner.
The dispute is recorded in al-Tatarakhaniyya.
2
Analyze the ruling on slave's crime
Review the rules for jinaaya of a slave during khiyar: if the seller has the option, the buyer (upon execution) chooses between surrender and ransom; if the buyer has the option, he cannot return the slave. Distinguish this from the corpse case where the corpse is not a defect.
The slave's crime is considered a defect giving the buyer an option; a corpse is not a defect.
3
Understand the ancillary rulings
Study the rulings on maintenance, disposition, increments, mortgage, and quittance during khiyar. Note that maintenance is obligatory on the buyer when the option is his, and quittance is invalid according to Abu Yusuf but valid according to Muhammad.
Ancillary rulings depend on who holds the option and the status of ownership.
4
Distinguish the exceptional purchases
Understand why the custodian of the Kaaba's purchase and the replacement of a weak waqf slave do not confer ownership: they are akin to endowments.
These exceptions show that khiyar principles do not apply to all transactions.
5
Apply the principles
Given a scenario: buyer purchases a house with 3-day option, finds a corpse on day 2. Determine liability under Imam and Sahibayn. Then consider if the buyer had a slave who committed a crime during the option period.
The key is identifying possession vs. ownership at the time of the event.

كتاب البيع باب خيار الشرط ۲۱ وبيع أورد من شراء متولي أمر الكعبة إذا اشترى عبداً لخدمتها وعبد الوقف إذا ضعف واشترى ببدله آخر لم يملكه المشتري لأنه من باب الأوقاف وكذا لا ترد التركة المستغرقة بالدين فإنها تخرج عن ملك الميت، ولا تدخل في ملك الورثة والغرماء للقيد المذكور. وأما حكم جناية العبد في مدة الخيار، فإن كان الخيار للبائع فأجاز البيع لم يكن مختاراً للفداء وخير المشتري بين الدفع والفداء، وإن ذي اليد عنه وعندهما على من يصير الملك له ولا يكون وجود القتيل عيباً فلا خيار للمشتري بخلاف جناية العبد المبيع فإنها عيب؛ كذا في التتارخانية. وقول الإمام ولا أصل له في الشرع» معناه في المعاوضة فلا يرد عليه المدبر إذا غصب وضمن الغاصب قيمته فإنه يملكه فقد اجتمع العوضان في ملك السيد لأنه ضمان جناية لا ضمان معاوضة ؛ كذا في المعراج وفتح القدير. ولكن يرد عليه باب السلم فإن المسلم إليه ملك رأس مال السلم والمسلم فيه فقد اجتمعا في المعاوضة. وأجيب بأن المسلم فيه دين لرب السلم في ذمة المسلم إليه فهو كالثمن يملكه البائع في ذمة المشتري وأورد المنافع والأجرة المعجلة ملكهما المؤجر. وأجيب بأنها معدومة فلا ملك البائع ، ولو تصرف المشتري في المبيع في مدة الخيار والخيار له جاز تصرفه اجماعاً ويكون إجازة منه اهـ. وفي الخلاصة أن زوائد المبيع موقوفة إن تم البيع كانت للمشتري وإن فسخ كانت للبائع اهـ. وفي جامع «قلت الخ» جواب عنه .
Book of Sale, Chapter on Stipulated Option. Page 21. Also, an objection is raised regarding the purchase by the custodian of the Kaaba: if he buys a slave for its service, or the waqf slave if he becomes weak and another is bought in exchange, the buyer does not own it because it is from the category of endowments. Similarly, the estate consumed by debts does not enter the ownership of the heirs or creditors due to the mentioned restriction. As for the ruling on the slave's crime (jinaaya) during the option period: if the option belongs to the seller and he executes the sale, he is not entitled to ransom, and the buyer is given the choice between surrendering the slave (for execution) and paying ransom. If the sale is rescinded, the seller is given the choice similarly. In the first case, the buyer is only given the choice if he chooses to execute the sale; if the buyer chooses to rescind, then the choice goes to the seller due to the defect that occurred in the seller's hand. If the crime occurred in the buyer's hand, then the seller retains his option; if he executes, ownership is established for the buyer from the time of contract, and he is given the choice between surrender and ransom. If the option is for the buyer and the slave commits a crime in his hand during the period, he cannot return him to the seller. If a house is sold with an option for one of them and a corpse is found inside, the blood money is on the 'aqila of the person in possession according to Imam, but according to the two (Sahibayn), it is on the one who will eventually own it. The presence of the corpse is not a defect, so the buyer has no option, unlike the slave's crime which is a defect. This is from al-Tatarakhaniyya. The statement of Imam 'there is no basis in Shari'a' means in exchange transactions, so it is not refuted by the case of a mudabbar slave: if he is usurped and the usurper pays his value, the usurper owns him, and the two compensations are united in the master's ownership because it is compensation for injury (daman jinaaya), not compensation for exchange. This is from al-Mi'raj and Fath al-Qadir. But the case of salam (advance payment) refutes it: the seller (muslam ilayh) owns both the capital and the commodity, so they are united in exchange. The response is that the commodity is a debt in the liability of the seller, like the price which the seller owns as a debt in the buyer's liability. Another objection regarding benefits and advance rent: the lessor owns both; the response is that they are non-existent, so there is no ownership, and when they arise, the lessee owns them. This is from al-Binaya. The restriction is to the sold item because the price leaves the buyer's ownership by consensus, as we explained. In al-Siraj al-Mulik al-Wahhaj: Maintenance is obligatory on the buyer by consensus when the option is for him because the sold item leaves the seller's ownership. If the buyer disposes of the sold item during the option period when the option is for him, his disposition is valid by consensus and is considered confirmation. In al-Khulasa: The increments of the sold item are suspended; if the sale is completed, they belong to the buyer; if rescinded, to the seller. In Jami' al-Fusulayn: If the buyer with an option mortgages for the price, the mortgage is valid. If it is said: In Jami' al-Fusulayn it is also mentioned that if the option is for the buyer and the seller gives him a quittance from the price, the quittance is not valid. In al-Tatarakhaniyya: It is narrated from Muhammad that it is valid, so then the mortgage should also not be valid. I say: Quittance depends on a debt, and there is no debt upon him because the price remains in his ownership; mortgage does not require the actual existence of a debt, as evidenced by its validity for a promised debt, and we have explained this in our glosses on Jami' al-Fusulayn. But it is transmitted after that that the invalidity of quittance is the view of Abu Yusuf. In al-Mi'raj: the lack of clarity... so no benefit is obtained from his words. His statement 'then the mortgage should also not be valid' is a corollary to his statement 'the quittance is not valid', and my statement 'I said...' is a response to it.
Source pages p. 21

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion on khiyar al-shart, focusing on liability for crimes committed by a slave sold with an option, and other special cases. The author begins with two exceptional purchases: the custodian of the Kaaba buying a slave for service, and replacing a weak waqf slave with another; in both, the buyer does not own the slave because they are akin to endowments. Similarly, an estate consumed by debts does not transfer to heirs or creditors. The main topic is the slave's jinaaya (homicide or injury) during khiyar. The ruling depends on who has the option and whether the sale is executed or rescinded. If the seller has the option, upon execution the buyer is given the choice to surrender the slave or pay ransom; if the sale is rescinded, the seller has that choice. The khilaf between Imam and the Sahibayn appears in the case of finding a corpse in a house sold with an option: Imam says blood money is on the 'aqila of the person in possession, while the Sahibayn say it is on the one who will eventually own the property. The presence of a corpse is not a defect, so no option arises, unlike the slave's crime which is a defect. The author then addresses objections to Imam's statement 'no basis in Shari'a': the case of mudabbar (where usurper pays value and owns him) is distinguished because it is compensation for injury, not exchange. The case of salam is answered by noting the commodity is a debt, not an actual property. The case of advance rent is answered by noting benefits are non-existent at contract. The passage concludes with rulings on maintenance, disposition, increments, mortgage, and quittance during khiyar, noting the differences between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad. This demonstrates how khiyar affects various ancillary rights and obligations.

Key Terms
جناية (jinaaya): A criminal act committed by a slave, typically homicide or bodily harm, leading to liability for blood money or ransom.
عاقلة ('aqila): The group of relatives (usually paternal male relatives) who are collectively responsible for paying blood money on behalf of the killer.
إبراء (ibra'): Quittance or release from a debt; here, the seller releasing the buyer from the price during khiyar.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is true about the corpse found in a house sold with khiyar?
A) The buyer can rescind the sale because the corpse is a defect.
B) The blood money is always on the seller's 'aqila according to all Hanafis.
C) Imam Abu Hanifa says the blood money is on the 'aqila of the person in possession.
D) The Sahibayn agree with Imam Abu Hanifa.

Key Takeaway
The intra-Hanafi dispute on blood money for a corpse found during khiyar hinges on possession (Imam) versus eventual ownership (Sahibayn), with no tarjih given in the passage.

No Ownership in Kaaba Slave and Debt-Overwhelmed Estate
Ruling on purchase for Kaaba service and estate consumed by debt

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that ownership does not transfer in two specific cases: purchases for Kaaba service (or waqf substitutes) and estates fully consumed by debts.

Core Idea
Ownership transfer is blocked when property is dedicated to a public trust or entirely consumed by debts.

This lesson presents two distinct rulings from Hanafi fiqh where the usual transfer of ownership upon sale or death is blocked. Understanding these exceptions clarifies the boundaries of property rights in Islamic law, especially in the context of waqf and debt repayment.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first case
Read the passage and locate the scenario where the custodian of the Kaaba buys a slave for its service. The ruling states that the buyer does not own the slave.
This is analogous to any purchase for a waqf's benefit.
2
Identify the second case
Find the case of an estate (tarikah) that is completely consumed by debts. The ruling says it leaves the deceased's ownership and does not pass to heirs or creditors.
The estate is used solely to settle debts.
3
Recognize the common principle
Both cases involve a legal impediment to ownership transfer: waqf status (first case) and debt priority (second case). In both, the property is not freely transferable.
The phrase 'due to the mentioned restriction' points to this principle.
4
State the legal effect
For the first case: the sale is invalid (batil) regarding ownership transfer; the slave becomes part of the waqf. For the second: the estate is not inherited; it is used for debt repayment.
Understanding these exceptions prevents incorrect assumptions about property transfers.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبيع أورد من شراء متولي أمر الكعبة إذا اشترى عبداً لخدمتها وعبد الوقف إذا ضعف واشترى ببدله آخر لم يملكه المشتري لأنه من باب الأوقاف وكذا لا ترد التركة المستغرقة بالدين فإنها تخرج عن ملك الميت، ولا تدخل في ملك الورثة والغرماء للقيد المذكور.
And a sale [issue] is reported from the purchase of the person in charge of the Kaaba: If he buys a slave for its service, and a waqf slave if it becomes weak and he buys another in its place, the buyer does not own it because it is from the category of awqaf. Similarly, the estate overwhelmed by debt is not returned; it leaves the ownership of the deceased and does not enter the ownership of the heirs and creditors due to the mentioned restriction.
Source pages p. 21

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling (hukm) on two specific cases where ownership does not transfer despite a sale or death. First: if the custodian of the Kaaba buys a slave for Kaaba service, or replaces a weak waqf slave with a new purchase, the buyer does not own the slave because it is considered part of the waqf (endowment) category—the new slave takes the legal status of the original waqf property. Second: an estate that is completely consumed by debts (i.e., all assets are needed to pay off debts) does not pass into the ownership of heirs or creditors; it leaves the deceased's ownership and is not acquired by anyone—it is simply used to settle debts. The phrase 'due to the mentioned restriction' refers to the principle that such property is not freely transferable. The rulings are based on the special nature of waqf and the priority of debt repayment over inheritance.

Key Terms
متولي أمر الكعبة (mutawallī amr al-ka'bah): The person entrusted with the affairs of the Kaaba; a custodian or caretaker.
التركة المستغرقة بالدين (al-tarikah al-mustaghraqah bi al-dayn): An estate that is entirely consumed by debts, leaving nothing for heirs.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If the custodian of the Kaaba buys a slave for the Kaaba's service, who owns the slave according to this passage?
A) The custodian owns it.
B) The Kaaba owns it.
C) No one owns it; it becomes part of the waqf.
D) The seller retains ownership.

Key Takeaway
Property dedicated to public trust (waqf) or fully consumed by debts does not transfer ownership in a normal way.

Liability for a Slave's Crime During the Option Period
Rulings on slave's crime when sale has option (khiyar)

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rulings for a slave's crime when the option is with the seller or buyer, and apply the rule to determine who bears liability.

Core Idea
When a slave commits a crime during khiyar, the option-holder's confirmation or rescission determines who chooses between handing over the slave or paying ransom.

This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling on a slave's crime (jinayat al-'abd) when the sale is subject to an option condition (khiyar al-shart). The liability and options depend on who holds the option and in whose possession the crime occurred. We will analyze the various scenarios presented in the text from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and option holder
Determine who has the option (seller or buyer) and in whose possession the crime occurred.
Possession may be with the seller before delivery or with the buyer after delivery.
2
Option belongs to seller
If the seller has the option, analyze two sub-scenarios: seller confirms sale vs. rescinds. If seller confirms, buyer chooses between handing over slave or paying ransom. If seller rescinds, seller gets that choice.
If crime occurs in buyer's possession and seller confirms, buyer's choice is from time of contract.
3
Option belongs to buyer
If the buyer has the option and the crime occurs in his possession during the option period, the buyer cannot return the slave; he must accept the slave and bear liability.
The sale becomes binding on the buyer.
4
Contrast with dead body ruling
Note that a dead body found in a house sold with khiyar is not a defect, whereas a slave's crime is a defect allowing return in certain scenarios.
This distinction affects the buyer's option to return.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما حكم جناية العبد في مدة الخيار، فإن كان الخيار للبائع فأجاز البيع لم يكن مختاراً للفداء وخير المشتري بين الدفع والفداء، وإن فسخ البيع خير البائع كذلك، وفي الأول إنما يخير المشتري بين الدفع والفداء إذا اختار إمضاء البيع فإن اختار المشتري فسخه فالخيار للبائع للعيب الحادث في يد البائع، فإن كانت في يد المشتري فالبائع على خياره، فإن أجاز ثبت الملك للمشتري من وقت العقد وخير بين الدفع والفداء فإن كان الخيار للمشتري فجنى في يده في مدته لم يكن له أن يرده على بائعه ولو بيعت دار بخيار لأحدهما فوجد فيها قتيل فالدية على عاقلة ذي اليد عنه وعندهما على من يصير الملك له ولا يكون وجود القتيل عيباً فلا خيار للمشتري بخلاف جناية العبد المبيع فإنها عيب؛ كذا في التتارخانية.
As for the ruling concerning the crime of a slave during the option period: if the option belongs to the seller and he confirms the sale, he is not given a choice regarding ransom, and the buyer is given the choice between handing over (the slave) or paying ransom. If the seller rescinds the sale, the seller is given the choice similarly. In the first case, the buyer is only given the choice between handing over and ransom if he chooses to execute the sale; if the buyer chooses to rescind, then the option goes to the seller because of the defect that occurred while in the seller's possession. If the crime occurred while in the buyer's possession, the seller still has his option; if he confirms, ownership is established for the buyer from the time of contract, and the buyer is given choice between handing over and ransom. If the option belongs to the buyer and the slave commits a crime while in his possession during the option period, he cannot return it to the seller. Even if a house is sold with an option for one of them, and a dead body is found in it, the blood money is on the clan of the person in possession according to Abu Hanifa, while according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) it is on the one who will ultimately own the property. The presence of a dead body is not a defect, so the buyer has no option (to return), unlike the crime of a sold slave which is a defect. This is as in al-Tatarkhaniyya.
Source pages p. 21

Commentary
This passage provides detailed rulings on the scenario when a sold slave commits a crime (such as homicide) during the option period (khiyar al-shart). The rulings depend on who holds the option and who has possession. Summary: (1) If the seller has option and confirms the sale, the seller is not liable for ransom; the buyer may choose to hand over the slave (to the victim's family) or pay ransom. (2) If the seller rescinds, the seller gets that choice. (3) If the buyer chooses to rescind after a crime in seller's hand, the option goes to seller due to a defect. (4) If crime occurs while buyer has possession (but still option for seller), seller still has option; if he confirms, buyer owns from contract time and gets the choice. (5) If buyer has option and slave commits crime, buyer cannot return the slave. Additionally, a separate ruling: if a house is sold with an option and a dead body is found, the blood money liability is on the clan of the possessor (Abu Hanifa) or on the ultimate owner (Sahibayn). The dead body is not a defect allowing return, unlike a slave's crime which is a defect. The passage cites al-Tatarkhaniyya as the source.

Key Terms
جناية العبد (jināyat al-ʿabd): A crime committed by a slave, such as homicide, which has financial consequences.
خيار الشرط (khiyār al-sharṭ): An option stipulated in a sale contract allowing one or both parties to rescind within a specified period.
فداء (fidāʾ): Ransom paid to the victim's family in lieu of handing over the slave.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If the seller has the option and the slave commits a crime while in the buyer's possession, what happens if the seller confirms the sale?
A) The buyer can return the slave.
B) The seller must pay the ransom.
C) The buyer owns the slave from the contract time and has the choice between handing over or ransom.
D) The seller has the choice between handing over or ransom.

Key Takeaway
A slave's crime during khiyar: if seller has option, buyer may have choice to return or pay; if buyer has option, he cannot return.

Blood Money for a Dead Body Found in a House Sold with Khiyar
Rulings on dead body found in house with khiyar, intra-Hanafi dispute on blood money

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the intra-Hanafi dispute on blood money for a dead body found in a house during khiyar, and apply the ruling to determine liability.

Core Idea
A dead body found in a house sold with khiyar is not a defect, but blood money liability differs between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn based on possession vs. ultimate ownership.

This lesson explores an additional ruling within the same passage: if a dead body is found in a house that was sold with an option (khiyar), who is liable for the blood money (diya)? The text records an intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and his two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani. Understanding this difference is crucial for applying the correct rule in practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
A house is sold with an option for one party. During the option period, a dead body is found inside.
The sale is not yet finalized.
2
State the dispute
Abu Hanifa: blood money is on the clan (aqila) of the person in possession (dhu al-yad). The two (Sahibayn): blood money is on the clan of the one who will ultimately own the property (man yasir al-mulk lahu).
The text does not provide a tarjih.
3
Determine practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
If the option is with the seller and the buyer has possession, then according to Abu Hanifa, the buyer's clan pays; according to the Sahibayn, the seller's clan pays because ownership will eventually pass to the seller if he rescinds.
The ruling affects financial liability.
4
Contrast with a slave's crime
The text emphasizes that a dead body is not a defect, so the buyer cannot return the house for that reason, unlike a slave's crime which is a defect.
This distinction is important for rescission rights.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The rulings depend on who holds the option and who has possession. عاقلة (ʿāqilah): The clan or group responsible for paying blood money (diya) on behalf of a member who caused death.
خيار الشرط (khiyār al-sharṭ): An option stipulated in a sale contract allowing one or both parties to rescind within a specified period.
قتيل (qatīl): A dead body (result of homicide).

Scope of the Maxim 'No Basis in Sharia' and Its Exceptions
Defines and defends the scope of a Hanafi maxim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi maxim 'لا أصل له في الشرع', identify its specific application to exchange contracts, and distinguish the three exceptional cases that do not violate it.

Core Idea
The maxim 'no basis in Sharia' applies only to exchange contracts; guarantees, salam, and rent do not violate it.

Imam Abu Hanifa's maxim 'لا أصل له في الشرع' (no basis in Sharia) is often invoked to invalidate transactions, but its scope is narrower than it seems. This lesson unpacks the maxim, showing it is limited to exchange contracts (mu'awadah) where both countervalues must not be owned by the same party at contract time. We will examine three hypothetical objections—mudabbar, salam, and rent—and see how Hanafi scholars resolve them without breaching the rule.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Maxim and Its Domain
Locate Imam Abu Hanifa's statement and confirm that the maxim applies exclusively to exchange contracts (mu'awadah). Note the Arabic phrase 'معناه في المعاوضة'.
2
Examine the First Exception: Mudabbar
Read the case of a mudabbar slave who is usurped and the usurper pays compensation. Understand why this is not an exchange—it is a guarantee for injury (daman jinaaya), not a bilateral commutation.
The master ends up owning both the slave and the compensation, but this is accidental, not contractual.
3
Analyze the Second Exception: Salam
Consider the objection that in a salam contract, the buyer owns both the capital and the future commodity. Follow the answer: the commodity is a debt in the seller's liability, not actual ownership, similar to how a seller owns the price as a debt.
This preserves the rule because both countervalues are not simultaneously owned in actuality.
4
Analyze the Third Exception: Rent (Usufruct)
Review the objection that a lessor owns both the rent and the usufruct. The answer: the usufruct is non-existent at contract time; it only comes into existence gradually and is owned by the lessee as it arises.
Thus no simultaneous ownership of both countervalues occurs.
5
Synthesize the Binding Principle
Conclude that the maxim only invalidates exchange contracts where both countervalues are corporeal, existent, and owned by the same party at the time of contract. The price remains in the buyer's ownership by consensus, so it is not an issue.
This precision prevents misapplication of the maxim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقول الإمام ولا أصل له في الشرع» معناه في المعاوضة فلا يرد عليه المدبر إذا غصب وضمن الغاصب قيمته فإنه يملكه فقد اجتمع العوضان في ملك السيد لأنه ضمان جناية لا ضمان معاوضة ؛ كذا في المعراج وفتح القدير. ولكن يرد عليه باب السلم فإن المسلم إليه ملك رأس مال السلم والمسلم فيه فقد اجتمعا في المعاوضة. وأجيب بأن المسلم فيه دين لرب السلم في ذمة المسلم إليه فهو كالثمن يملكه البائع في ذمة المشتري وأورد المنافع والأجرة المعجلة ملكهما المؤجر. وأجيب بأنها معدومة فلا ملك لها وإذا حدثت ملكها المستأجر ؛ كذا في البناية. قيد بالمبيع لأن الثمن لا يخرج عن ملك المشتري اجماعاً كما بيناه.
And the statement of the Imam: 'There is no basis for it in the Sharia' means in exchange (mu'awadah). So it is not objected by the case of a mudabbar: if he is usurped and the usurper guarantees his value, then he owns him, for the two countervalues have come together in the ownership of the master because it is a guarantee for injury, not a guarantee for exchange. This is as in al-Mi'raj and Fath al-Qadir. But the chapter of salam does object: the person to whom salam is made owns the capital of salam and the salam commodity, so both countervalues have come together in exchange. The answer is that the salam commodity is a debt owed by the salam seller to the salam buyer, so it is like the price which the seller owns in the buyer's liability. And the objection regarding usufruct and advance rent: the lessor owns both. The answer is that they are non-existent, so there is no ownership; when they come into existence, the lessee owns them. This is as in al-Binaya. The restriction is to the sold item because the price does not leave the buyer's ownership by consensus, as we have explained.
Source pages p. 21

Commentary
This passage clarifies a statement attributed to Imam Abu Hanifa regarding the rule that 'there is no basis for it in the Sharia' (لا أصل له في الشرع), which is used to invalidate certain transactions. The Imam intended this rule specifically for exchange contracts (mu'awadah) where both countervalues (price and object) must not be owned by the same person at the time of contract. The passage addresses three potential objections: (1) Mudabbar slave (one whose freedom is postponed after master's death): if usurped and the usurper pays the value, the master ends up owning both the slave and the compensation. But this is not an exchange; it is a guarantee for injury (daman jinaaya), so it does not violate the rule. (2) Salam (forward sale): the salam buyer owns both the capital and the commodity? The reply is that the commodity is a debt in the seller's liability, not actual ownership, so it is analogous to the price owed. (3) Advanced rent for usufruct: the lessor owns both the rent and the usufruct? The reply is that the usufruct is non-existent at the time of contract; ownership arises later for the lessee. Thus these are not true exchanges. The rule is restricted to the sold item (mabi'), because the price remains in the buyer's ownership by consensus until delivery.

Key Terms
لا أصل له في الشرع (lā aṣla lahu fī al-sharʿ): A legal maxim meaning a transaction has no basis in Sharia and is therefore invalid, particularly in exchange contracts.
معاوضة (muʿāwaḍah): An exchange contract where one countervalue (price) is given for another (object); bilateral commutative contract.
مدبر (mudabbar): A slave whose master declares that he will be free upon the master's death.
ضمان جناية (ḍamān jināyah): A guarantee for injury or tort, not a contractual exchange.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is a salam contract not an objection to the maxim 'لا أصل له في الشرع'?
A) Because salam is not a binding contract.
B) Because the salam commodity is considered a debt, not an actual owned object at contract time.
C) Because the maxim applies only to sales of existing items.
D) Because the buyer never actually owns the capital.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi maxim 'no basis in Sharia' is precise: it invalidates only exchange contracts where both countervalues are actually owned by one party at contract time; guarantees, salam, and rent are excluded because one countervalue is a debt or non-existent.

Four Sub-Rulings for the Buyer During Khiyar
Rulings on maintenance, disposal, increments, and pledge for buyer during khiyar

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four rulings (maintenance, disposal, increments, pledge) that apply to a buyer holding khiyar and explain the legal reasoning behind each.

Core Idea
During khiyar, the buyer bears maintenance, may dispose validly, increments are suspended, and pledge is allowed.

This lesson examines four specific rulings that apply to the buyer during the option period (khiyar). These rulings cover maintenance expenses, the buyer's power to dispose of the item, the status of any increments, and the permissibility of pledging the price. Each ruling is derived from the buyer's legal interest in the item and is either established by consensus or by logical extension.

Guided Path
1
Identify the buyer's responsibility for maintenance (nafaqah)
Why is maintenance obligatory? Because the item has left the seller's possession and the buyer has the beneficial interest during khiyar.
This is by consensus (ijma).
2
Analyze the effect of disposal (tasarruf) during khiyar
Disposal of the sold item by the buyer is valid and counts as acceptance (ijazah) of the sale, even without verbal consent.
Also by consensus.
3
Determine the status of increments (zawa'id)
Increments (e.g., offspring, growth) are suspended (mawquf); ownership follows the outcome of the sale.
If sale finalized, increments go to buyer; if rescinded, to seller.
4
Evaluate the permissibility of pledging (rahn) the price
The buyer can pledge the price (thaman) as collateral for a debt; such a pledge is valid.
This is based on extension of ownership interest.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي السراج ملك الوهاج والنفقة تجب على المشتري بالإجماع إذا كان الخيار له بخروج المبيع عن البائع ، ولو تصرف المشتري في المبيع في مدة الخيار والخيار له جاز تصرفه اجماعاً ويكون إجازة منه اهـ. وفي الخلاصة أن زوائد المبيع موقوفة إن تم البيع كانت للمشتري وإن فسخ كانت للبائع اهـ. وفي جامع الفصولين : المشتري بالخيار لو رهن بالثمن رهناً جاز الرهن به اهـ.
And in al-Siraj Malak al-Wahhaj: Maintenance (nafaqah) is obligatory on the buyer by consensus if the option is his, because the sold item has left the seller's ownership. If the buyer disposes of the sold item during the option period and the option is his, his disposal is valid by consensus and it constitutes acceptance (from him). In al-Khulasa: The increments of the sold item are suspended; if the sale is completed, they belong to the buyer; if rescinded, they belong to the seller. In Jami' al-Fusulayn: The buyer with the option, if he pledges the price as a collateral, the pledge is valid.
Source pages p. 21

Commentary
This passage presents four distinct rulings related to the buyer who holds the khiyar (option) in a sale: (1) Maintenance expenses (nafaqah) of the sold item are the buyer's responsibility by consensus, because the item has left the seller's ownership and entered the buyer's beneficial possession. (2) The buyer may dispose of the sold item (e.g., sell it) during the option period; such disposal is valid and is considered an acceptance of the sale, i.e., he exercises his option to finalize the purchase. (3) Increments (zawa'id) that arise from the sold item (e.g., fruit, offspring, growth) are suspended; their ownership depends on whether the sale is finalized or rescinded. If the sale is completed, they belong to the buyer; if rescinded, to the seller. (4) The buyer may pledge (rahn) the price (thaman) as collateral for a debt; such a pledge is valid. These rulings are sourced from later Hanafi works and are mufta bihi (operative).

Key Terms
النفقة (al-nafaqah): Maintenance expenses such as feeding, housing, and care for the sold item (e.g., an animal or slave) during the option period.
زوائد المبيع (zawāʾid al-mabīʿ): Increments (e.g., profit, offspring, growth) that arise from the sold item during the option period.
إجازة (ijāzah): Acceptance or confirmation of a sale, often by conduct (e.g., disposing of the item).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What happens to the increments (zawa'id) of the sold item if the sale is later rescinded during khiyar?
A) The buyer keeps them.
B) They are shared equally.
C) They go to the seller.
D) They are given to charity.

Key Takeaway
During khiyar, the buyer is responsible for maintenance, any disposal confirms the sale, increments are suspended pending finalization, and pledging the price is valid.
